#### And, if they win policymaking good endorse the aff without the justifications we're critiquing

Philippe **Bourbeau 15**, Associate Professor, holder of the Canada
Research Chair in Immigration and Security, in the Department of
Political Science at the University Laval, Ph.D. from the University of
British Columbia, Juha A. Vuori, Professor of World Politics at the
University of Helsinki, Adjunct Professor of International Politics at
the University of Tampere, 11/16/15, "Security, resilience and
desecuritization: multidirectional moves and dynamics," Critical Studies
on Security, Vol. 3, No. 3, p. 257-259

Securitization and desecuritization ¶ The ceteris paribus normative
push, and political recommendation of the securitization approach has
been 'less security, more politics', and the development of 'possible
modalities' for [the [desecuritization]{.mark} of politics]{.underline}
(Wæver 1989a, 52): it [[is]{.underline}]{.mark} generally (which can
only be assessed in practice though) [more [conducive to treat
identities as identities]{.mark}, religion as religion, the [environment
as the environment]{.mark}, and so on, and [to
engage]{.mark}]{.underline} their [[politics through]{.mark} the
particular modalities and [rationalities of those fields **rather
than**]{.mark} **those [of security]{.mark}**]{.underline}. In the
received reading, whilst securitization raises issues into the realm of
security policies and practices (arrow 2a in Figure 1),
[desecuritization lowers issues back into the realm of 'regular
politics' or removes issues from the political agenda
altogether]{.underline} (arrow 1 in Figure 1). ¶ [[Desecuritization can
be achieved]{.mark} through a number of options: [by]{.mark} simply
**[not talking]{.mark} about issues [in terms of security]{.mark}**, by
[keeping responses]{.mark} to securitized issues [in forms that **do not
create security dilemmas**]{.mark}]{.underline} [or other **vicious
spirals**]{.underline}, [[and]{.mark} by [moving]{.mark} security
[issues back into 'normal politics']{.mark}]{.underline} (Wæver 2000,
253). These options can follow objectivist, constructivist or
deconstructivist strategies in bringing about desecuritization (Huysmans
(1995, 65--67). These strategies differ in regard to how the process
relates to the claimed threat: has the threat been dealt with, can the
security drama be somehow handled from without, or can identities beyond
security threats be produced from within the process. ¶ [Beyond
conceptualizing desecuritization as an option or a strategy, it has also
been viewed from the viewpoint of political actors]{.underline} (de
Wilde 2008, 597), [and their political moves in games of contestation
and resistance]{.underline} (Vuori 2011a, 2015). [There can be
[desecuritizing actors]{.mark} who [**evade**, circumvent or]{.mark}
**directly [oppose securitizing moves]{.mark}**]{.underline} by, for
example, emphasizing competing threats (de Wilde 2008, 597). Security
policies aim at desecuritization (the solution to the threatening
situation), but desecuritization can also happen independently from the
actions of securitizing or desecuritizing actors: the original security
problem may be solved, institutions may adapt through new reproductive
structures, discourses may change (e.g., with the loss of interest or
audiences), and the original referent object may be lost (de Wilde
2008). ¶ [A key issue of debate has been on whether desecuritization can
be considered to be an active political process, or whether
desecuritization can only happen as a fading away of the
issue]{.underline} (Behnke 2006, 65): the question is whether the logic
and possibility of securitization is necessarily retained in explicit
discussions of whether an issue has retained the status of a security
issue. [As empirical studies of securitization and desecuritization
dynamics]{.underline} (e.g., Salter and Mutlu 2013; Lupovici 2014; Vuori
2015; Donnelly 2015) have shown, [it is difficult to point to a
definitive end point for either securitization or desecuritization:
political and social situations evolve]{.underline}. Whichever the
philosophical stance on how and whether desecuritization can be achieved
(Vuori 2011a), such [empirical studies show that political actors do
make **active desecuritization moves**]{.underline}. ¶ Indeed,
systematizing empirical studies of desecuritization, Hansen (2012, 529;
539--545) has identified four ideal type forms for the concept. In
regard to its issues of concern, namely the status of enmity and the
possibility of a public sphere, when a larger conflict is still within
the realm of possibility, but [when a particular issue is presented with
terms other than security, we have an instance of (1) 'change through
**stabilisation**]{.underline}' (arrow 2b in Figure 1); when another
issue takes the place of a previously securitized issue, [we have (2)
'replacement'; when the originally phrased **threat is resolved**, we
have]{.underline} (3) **[rearticulation]{.underline}**; and
finally[,]{.underline} when potentially insecure subjects are
marginalized through depoliticization, we have (4) 'silencing' (types
2--3 are represented by arrow 1 in Figure 1). ¶ The previous literature
on both securitization (arrow 2a in Figure 1) and desecuritization
(arrow 1 in Figure 1) has produced ample illustrations of both dynamics.
As a brief example of how both dynamics can alter between the same
political actors, we can use some of the vicissitudes of Sino-Soviet
relations. ¶ China entered the Cold War in the Soviet camp and relied on
the Soviet Union as the guarantee of the international security of the
new People's Republic. Chinese views in the late 1940s clearly
structured the world into two opposing camps, with China firmly in the
Soviet one (Mao 1949). In the 1950s, however, Sino-Soviet relations
soured, and the following 'Sino-Soviet split' (Lüthi 2008) has been used
as an example of the capacity of 'parochial' securitizations to become
disaffected by or even be withdrawn from dominant 'macrosecuritizations'
(Buzan and Wæver 2009, 257). ¶ Following the split, Sino-Soviet
relations in the 1960s were characterized by intensive ideological
conflict, and Mao Zedong securitized Soviet revisionism as a major
threat for the Chinese Communist Party (Vuori 2011b). Indeed, newly
available documents suggest that it was the Chinese side, in effect Mao
Zedong, which was more active in the pursuit of ideological conflict
(Lüthi 2008, 2). In his securitization of the Soviet Union, Mao linked
the revisionism he identified there to that which he also securitized
domestically (Vuori 2011b), and the issue of revisionism was presented
as an issue of life and death for the party. ¶ Sino-Soviet relations
began to mend in the 1980s with the removal of a number of political
obstacles and with the intensification of the conflict between the
United States and the Soviet Union (Wishnick 2001). Yet, it is only with
the fall of the Soviet Union that we can see an overall desecuritization
in the form of rearticulation (Hansen 2012, 542--544) taking place in
Sino-Russian relations. In the aftermath of the end of the Cold War,
China's line was not to take the lead in international affairs. China
worked towards 'world multipolarization', which was exemplified with
China and Russia forming a 'strategic partnership' in 1996. China and
Russia even shared the same 'threat package' of 'terrorism, separatism,
and religious extremism' (the 'three evils') within the Shanghai
Cooperation Organization (Jackson 2006, 310). These are strong
indicators of how the two states have managed to reform their identities
away from the Sino-Soviet antagonism. In the overall state relations
then, we can see a rearticulative desecuritization tactic at play on
both sides: ever since the early 1980s, China's policy towards the
Soviet Union (and later Russia) shifted from antagonism to one of
collaboration and negotiation rather than securitization.¶
[[Desecuritization can be conceptualized]{.underline}]{.mark} in the
above manner [[as a **negative ontological corollary** to
securitization]{.underline}]{.mark}. Yet, [it is also prudent to
investigate securitization and desecuritization as political moves in
order to potentially understand the logic of when and how they are
wielded in **practical politics**]{.underline}. It is proposed here that
in addition to instances where a securitized situation is dismantled
(arrow 1 in Figure 1), [desecuritization can also be [viewed as a
political move]{.mark} that can be [deployed before 'securitization
plays' in a game]{.mark}]{.underline} of securitization (arrow 2a in
Figure 1). In other words, [desecuritization moves]{.underline} --
[[both in terms of **discourse** and **practice**]{.underline}]{.mark}
-- [can be used in a pre-emptive manner before the threshold of
securitization is reached]{.underline} (arrow 2b in Figure 1). For Wæver
(2000, 254), silencing can be a strategy to 'pre-empt or forestall
securitization'. We argue here that beyond silencing, **[[active]{.mark}
desecuritization [efforts can]{.mark} be made to [block]{.mark} the
[escalation]{.mark} of a contention]{.underline}**. Thereby, in addition
to change through stabilization (Hansen 2012) and the silencing of an
issue (Wæver 2000), [**[there can be explicit rebuttals of security
frames]{.mark} and claims [before they are solidified into
policy]{.mark}**. This tactic can be termed 'pre-emptive
desecuritization through rebuttal'.]{.underline}

### Framework\-\--Narratives

#### Narratives [matter]{.underline}. They are [reflective]{.underline} of broader political and cultural contexts, and [legitimate]{.underline} systems of power. 

Jan **Daniel and** Jakub **Eberle 21**, Eberle is senior researcher at
the Institute of International Relations Prague in the Czech Republic
and Daniel is researcher and the Head of the Centre for the Study of
Global Regions at the Institute of International Relations, 2021,
"Speaking of hybrid warfare: Multiple narratives and differing expertise
in the 'hybrid warfare' debate in Czechia," Cooperation and Conflict,
Vol. 56, Issue 4, pp. 432-453,
<https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/00108367211000799>

Knowing the threat: narratives and security knowledge

[**Narrative analysis** has]{.underline} been widely [employed in **IR**
and **security studies** to explore how]{.underline} certain [issues are
understood and communicated or what]{.underline} political [consequences
of telling a]{.underline} particular [**narrative** there may
be]{.underline} (Bliesemann de Guevara, 2016; Krebs, 2015; Miskimmon et
al., 2014, 2017; Neumann, 2002; Spencer, 2016). Employing diverse
theoretical approaches, all these **[studies]{.underline}** nevertheless
**[concur]{.underline}** that [a well-crafted **narrative** represents a
**potent way** of rendering]{.underline} unfamiliar [phenomena
**understandable** and **presentable**]{.underline} to a wider audience
(White, 1990). [Building on]{.underline} these insights as well as those
of **[securitisation theory]{.underline}** (Buzan et al., 1998; Ördén,
2019), [we propose a **generalisable**]{.underline} heuristic
**[framework]{.underline}** [for the study of **security narratives** in
IR]{.underline}, together with their underlying knowledge, based around
the four elements of threat, protected value, response and knowledge.

[Narratives are **stories** told by people]{.underline} in order [to
make sense of their **practices** and the **problems** they
**face**]{.underline} (Bueger, 2013; Selbin, 2010; Wagenaar, 2011).
[Although a]{.underline} given [situation might not]{.underline}
necessarily [have]{.underline} a [meaning and **cohesiveness** in
itself, a **narrative** helps]{.underline} to [craft a **coherent
interpretation** -- a story -- that reduces]{.underline} any
[**complexity** and **uncertainty** that the situation might be posing
and **reconstructs** the **identity** of the **narrator**]{.underline}
(Homolar, 2011; Subotić, 2016; Wagenaar, 2011: 215). Thus, [narratives
assemble]{.underline} heterogenous elements (protagonists, practices,
wider structures and conditions influencing behaviour) into [a **larger
story**]{.underline}, articulated along particularly defined causes and
effects (Bueger and Gadinger, 2018: 74; Edkins, 2019; Homolar and
Rodríguez-Merino, 2019). [This]{.underline} also [means]{.underline}
that [narratives can speak]{.underline} ostensibly [about the **same
issue**]{.underline}, yet establish very different links between the
presented causes, effects and prospective actions, leading to stories
[with]{.underline} markedly **[different meanings]{.underline}**
(Stampnitzky, 2014: 6--7).

[Narratives are articulated from]{.underline} the broader
[knowledge]{.underline} that is [available to their **producers**,
reflecting what is **accepted** and **valued** in]{.underline} their
respective [**cultural** and **political** **contexts**]{.underline}
(Bueger, 2013; Jasanoff, 2007; Wagenaar, 2011).2 [The]{.underline}
particular types of **[knowledge]{.underline}** that are [drawn upon
serve as **prisms** through which a **policy issue** is
**constructed**]{.underline} in a certain way [and]{.underline} they
also [**stabilise** the **narrative links**]{.underline} between what
are presented as the main problems and their causes (Stampnitzky, 2014:
6--7). [For example, the story of]{.underline} the [conflict in
**Afghanistan** and policies proposed to **'solve' it** are very
different depending on whether they are weaved]{.underline} together
[from]{.underline} academic knowledge of [**Afghan tribal politics**,
or]{.underline} articulated from **[military counterinsurgency
doctrines]{.underline}** (Berling, 2019). While knowledge provides the
basis for the construction of a narrative, its manifestation as
[expertise]{.underline} -- socially recognised and authoritative
proficiency in certain issues -- [plays an important role]{.underline}
also [for the **legitimation** of the narrative's reading of a
problem]{.underline} (Berling and Bueger, 2015: 7; Boswell, 2008).
Therefore, we pay attention to what sorts of knowledge provide a basis
for narrative constructions of HW and what types of expertise are
articulated to give them credibility.

Building on previously outlined narrative characteristics, we propose a
generalisable framework to reconstruct the different narratives in the
Czech debate on HW on the basis of the main threat (its form and
location), the main threatened value and the main action needed to
counter the threat and protect the threatened value. In other words, as
Stavrianakis (2020: 233) succinctly put it in her analysis of risk
discourses, we explore how [particular narratives explain
'where]{.underline} potential [harm **comes from**, to **whom** or
**what**, and **how**?' and 'who has the **ability** to generate a
**constituency** **to act'**]{.underline}. Together, [these constitute
a]{.underline} more or less [**coherent plot** linking the **security
problem** with its **causes**, potential negative consequences for those
that need to be **protected** and a **desirable reaction** to avoid
**these consequences**]{.underline} (Bueger and Gadinger, 2018: 74). In
the final step, we also add a fourth element: the type of knowledge and
expertise these narratives put forward.

### Framework\-\--Geopolitical Imaginations

#### Evaluate competing [geopolitical imaginations]{.underline}. 

Jakub **Eberle and** Jan **Daniel 22**, Eberle is senior researcher at
the Institute of International Relations Prague in the Czech Republic
and Daniel is researcher and the Head of the Centre for the Study of
Global Regions at the Institute of International Relations, 01/01/2022,
"Anxiety Geopolitics: Hybrid Warfare, Civilisational Geopolitics, and
the Janus-Faced Politics of Anxiety," Political Geography, vol. 92, p.
102502,
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0962629821001621

[Our research is]{.underline} located [in **critical geopolitics**, from
which we borrow]{.underline} the [understanding]{.underline} of
[geopolitics as **discourses** and]{.underline} [**practices** through
which people ''**spatialize**' international politics and represent it
as a '**world**']{.underline} characterized by particular types of
places, peoples and dramas' (Ó Tuathail & Agnew, 1992, p. 192).
[**Critical geopolitics** focuses on how **political events** are
**articulated** as somehow related to]{.underline} or driven by
[**geographic phenomena**, or how issues are
'**geopoliticised**',]{.underline} that is [constructed as
**geopolitical problems**]{.underline} (Cadier, 2019, p. 71). [These
ideas are]{.underline} best [captured by]{.underline} the concept of
[**geopolitical imagination**: 'Geopolitical imaginations are the result
of **subjects'** attempt to **make sense** of the **world** by
associating **political values** with]{.underline} various [parts of
that **map**. They can also]{.underline} be spoken of [in the
**collective sense**, in which a group can]{.underline} be said to [have
**similar**]{.underline} (if ultimately unique) [**visions** of the
**world**.]{.underline}' (Dittmer & Dodds, 2008, p. 447) More recently,
[critical geopolitics]{.underline} has [paid]{.underline} increased
[attention to **affects** and **emotions**]{.underline} (Gökarıksel &
Secor, 2020; Laketa, 2019; Müller, 2013; Pain, 2009).1 Such [affective
geopolitics explores how **affects** are an **indivisible part** of
**social construction** of **space**, as they are]{.underline}
intimately [entangled with **languages** and **images** through which
our '**mental maps**' are **produced** and
**disseminated**]{.underline}. Summarising this broader argument, Laketa
argues that [geopolitics should be understood 'both as **socially
produced** and **intimately experienced**']{.underline} and,
consequently, 'the emotional and the affective become the site of the
geopolitical' and vice versa (Laketa, 2019, pp. 156, 160). Therefore,
[international politics is **spatialised**]{.underline} also [by
engaging subjects at the **affective level**, which is an **essential
part** of the production of **geopolitical imaginations** through which
**subjects view** the **world** and their **place within it**. This
links **critical geopolitics** to]{.underline} a range of [literatures
that deal with]{.underline} the relationship between [**identity**,
**emotions** and **affects**, including]{.underline} the scholarship on
[**ontological security** and **anxiety**]{.underline} in IR.

The rapidly growing [literature on **o**ntological **s**ecurity is
concerned with how **subjects**]{.underline} -- individuals, groups, or
states -- [deal with]{.underline} the **[uncertainty]{.underline}** of
modern life [without losing]{.underline} the '[security of the **self**,
the]{.underline} subjective [sense of **who one is**']{.underline}
(Mitzen, 2006, p. 344) that defines ontological security and makes life
bearable. [Building]{.underline} mainly [upon]{.underline} the work of
Anthony **[Giddens]{.underline}** (1991)[,]{.underline} classical
[contributions agree]{.underline} that [ontological security is
**sought** through]{.underline} an active process of [constructing the
**self** and **anchoring it**]{.underline} in the social world, which
happens [through]{.underline} the establishment of
[routines]{.underline} (Mitzen, 2006), creation of [biographical
narratives]{.underline} (Kinnvall, 2004; Steele, 2008; Subotić, 2016),
[or]{.underline} maintenance of [relationships]{.underline}
(Berenskoetter & Giegerich, 2010). [The key]{.underline} underlying
[purpose]{.underline} of ontological security-seeking [is to avoid
**anxiety**]{.underline}, which Giddens, following Freud, understands as
an elusive and paralysing 'generalised state of \[...\] emotions' that
is different from fear (Giddens, 1991, p. 44). [Fear is]{.underline} 'a
basic emotion [directed at a **specific object**]{.underline} that
prompts an adaptive response: fight or flight.' (Kinnvall & Mitzen,
2020, p. 241). In contrast, '[anxiety is **diffuse**, it is
**free-floating**: lacking a **specific object'**, which makes it
possible to **'pin' it** to **different things** and
**concepts**]{.underline} (Giddens, 1991, p. 44).
[Repressing]{.underline} the elusive [**anxiety** by]{.underline} this
'[pinning']{.underline}, which transforms it into the manageable fear of
something, [is]{.underline} then [one]{.underline} of the **[key
motive]{.underline}**s [of **human behaviour**]{.underline}. [As
**anxiety** is]{.underline} considered [overwhelming]{.underline} and
paralysing[; the '**chaos'** that 'threatens]{.underline} on the other
side of the ordinariness of the [everyday conventions']{.underline}
(1991, p. 37)[, subjects]{.underline} often prefer to [deal with it
through]{.underline} the [production of **fear** via]{.underline} the
discursive [**construction** of '**specific objects** and
**threats'**]{.underline} (Chernobrov, 2019, p. 39). [This involves
reaching to]{.underline} well-worn [**identity discourses**,
which]{.underline} serve as vehicles for ['\[t\]ransform]{.underline}ing
the [**anxiety** of the **unknown** into the **security** of the
**known**]{.underline} (recognizable, even if illusory) \[that\] affirms
the identity of the perceiving subject and enables it to confidently
interact with the international other.' (ibid.)

These arguments have a direct purchase for critical geopolitics, as
[**geopolitical imaginations** are good **examples** of how such
**sense-making** and **anxiety-managing discourses**]{.underline} can
[look like]{.underline}. Some have already recognised and developed this
link. For Guzzini, [the 'revival of **geopolitical thought**' in Europe
is]{.underline} directly [linked to **ontological
insecurity**]{.underline} stemming [from]{.underline} 'the sense of
[**disorientation** and]{.underline} foreign policy **[identity
crises]{.underline}** which followed 1989.' (Guzzini, 2016, pp. 14--15)
Making a more explicit link between anxiety and fear, Browning develops
a very similar argument: ['The attraction of **tropes** of a **new Cold
War**]{.underline} and a return of geopolitics [is]{.underline}
precisely [that they solve **anxiety**]{.underline} about current events
[by fitting them into]{.underline} clearly [**established systems** of
**meaning**,]{.underline} though doing so entails reducing anxiety by
[**emphasising** a **world** of **threats** and **fears**']{.underline}
(Browning, 2018, p. 113).

### Framework\-\--Hybrid War

#### Hybrid war discourse has "real life effects" and fosters [creeping securitization]{.underline}. 

Jan **Daniel and** Jakub **Eberle 21**, Eberle is senior researcher at
the Institute of International Relations Prague in the Czech Republic
and Daniel is researcher and the Head of the Centre for the Study of
Global Regions at the Institute of International Relations, 2021,
"Speaking of hybrid warfare: Multiple narratives and differing expertise
in the 'hybrid warfare' debate in Czechia," Cooperation and Conflict,
Vol. 56, Issue 4, pp. 432-453,
<https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/00108367211000799>

Conclusion

[This **article** analyses how]{.underline} the threat of [**HW** is
**constructed**]{.underline} in the Czech public debate. Connecting
narrative analysis with attention to the role of knowledge and expertise
in making authoritative claims on security issues, [we
argue]{.underline} that the [broader **HW discourse** can be
**disentangled** into **three narratives** that link the **exact
nature** of the **main threat**, **protected value** and **response** in
different ways]{.underline}. Furthermore, we show how [different bodies
of knowledge provide a basis for **differing ways** of **narrating** and
**justifying** a **specific understanding** of **HW**.]{.underline}
While [the literature]{.underline} has [already criticised the
**vagueness** and **broadness** of the **HW label**]{.underline}, we
highlight how this vagueness accommodates different readings of the main
threat. We show that HW is a very different issue when interpreted
through the lens of military science, popular literature on Russian
history or cognitive psychology.

The [**disentangling** of]{.underline} the [**HW discourse** matters
for]{.underline} both future analyses of HW and [**'real-life' effects**
of the discourse. As nearly **anything** that has a **vague reference**
to dangers in the **information domain**, challenges posed by
**illiberalism** or **great-power politics** can be attached to the **HW
label**; it is difficult to know what **HW points** to exactly, and
where the **threat** is **located**]{.underline} precisely. This is
unhelpful both for a sound risk analysis and for an informed and
critical public debate. It also has profound political implications. [An
unproblematised reception of]{.underline} the **[HW]{.underline}**
discourse [contributes to]{.underline} the **[creeping
securitisation]{.underline}** [of issues that would have
not]{.underline} necessarily [been linked to **security**]{.underline}
otherwise, [such as **media literacy** or **public
diplomacy**]{.underline} (Szostek, 2020). [Proliferation of **security
framings**]{.underline} of different issues in the name of HW
[can]{.underline}, furthermore, [lead to **mutually undermining** and
**contradicting policy reactions**]{.underline}. It makes a difference
whether we treat the public as critically thinking citizens that can
(with some assistance) make up their own minds, or if we understand them
as a passive mass that needs to be protected from even being reached by
hostile messaging or disinformation (see also Ördén, 2019). Each implies
a markedly different view of the citizen -- as active subject shaping
their own life versus passive object in need of protection -- and her
relation to the government. Similarly, [it matters whether we
understand]{.underline} our [adversaries as **rational actors** that can
be **deterred** or **accommodated**,]{.underline} as the defence
narrative does, [or as **malign** and inherently **irrational
'others'**]{.underline} [with whom we share no **common rules or

Rühle, 2019).

#### The discourse of "[hybrid war]{.underline}" maintains NATO and the West's [identity]{.underline}. 

Maria **Mälksoo 18**, Senior Lecturer in International Security at the
Brussels School of International Studies at the University of Kent,
2018, "Countering hybrid warfare as ontological security management: the
emerging practices of the EU and NATO," European Security, Vol. 27,
Issue 3, pp. 374-392,
<https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/09662839.2018.1497984>

[A spectre]{.underline} is [haunt]{.underline}ing [the

"political warfare", described by Mark Galeotti (2016b) as a "21st

and fake news are taking the place of planes, bombs and missiles".
Similar destabilisation campaigns have been noticed in the context of
the French presidential election and in the build-up to German federal
elections in 2017, and in numerous other European states.2

While the "new wars" debate has a long pedigree in international studies

on]{.underline} the [potential of]{.underline} these [wars to generate
**immaterial insecurity effects**. This article offers an **ontological
security**]{.underline}-situated [**reading** on the]{.underline} added
[insights]{.underline} the notion "**[hybrid warfare]{.underline}**"
[brings to bear for our understanding of the]{.underline} contemporary
[Western]{.underline} security [predicament. **Ontological
security**]{.underline} (OS) is a condition [underpin]{.underline}ning
[the actor's ability to **act** in the **world** with]{.underline} basic
[confidence about how the **world works**]{.underline} and her own place
within it.3 **[Ontological insecurity]{.underline}**, in turn,
[signifies "the **deep**, incapacitating **state** of not knowing how to
**get by** in the **world**]{.underline}" (Mitzen and Schweller 2011, p.
29). [With an]{.underline} explicit [emphasis on **uncertainty** and

[the]{.underline} disturbing [ripple effect of **hybrid

[I]{.underline} propose to [link]{.underline} the study of
[OS]{.underline} in IR systematically [with]{.underline} the debates on
[hybrid warfare along **ontological**, **epistemological**, and

[Western responses]{.underline} to, the said challenge. An OS

and routinised practices (as modes to confront anxiety in order to

argument is that "**[hybrid warfare]{.underline}**" [capsizes an
embedded **cognitive structure** about what **war is**]{.underline},
thus [defying]{.underline} [attempts of **organising life** and **social
relations** in a **particular way**, with]{.underline} fundamental
[consequences for the **OS** of]{.underline} the European Union (EU) and
**[NATO]{.underline}**. I proceed from the premise that defending and
[promoting a]{.underline} particular [vision of one's self is important

[Western]{.underline} security-political [institutions, and a

agency]{.underline} (Flockhart 2016, p. 801). [This article offers an
**exploration** of]{.underline} the EU and [NATO's **identity
maintenance**]{.underline} "especially [by **acting**]{.underline}, or
doing things" (Mitzen and Schweller 2011, p. 28) [vis-à-vis **hybrid
threats.**]{.underline} It thus subscribes to Flockhart's suggestion
that the two strategies of OS maximisation (a "strategy of being" and a
"strategy of doing") are interlinked and cannot be understood in
isolation from one another (Flockhart 2016, p. 799, p. 816).

[What]{.underline} authority [claims are]{.underline} the EU and [NATO
making about their **ability** and **competence** to handle **hybrid

## Perm / Link

### Perm\-\--2NC

#### Utilitarian appeals to rehabilitate security occlude resisting its foundations

Chris **Rossdale 16**, Teaching Fellow, International Relations,
University of Warwick, "Activism, resistance and security," Chapter 14
of *Ethical Security Studies: A new research agenda*, eds. Nyman and
Burke, 2016, no page \#

The previous two sections have highlighted a number of ways in which
practices of resistance and activism engage the relationship between
ethics and security in different ways. [In]{.underline} producing
subjugated knowledges, revealing the exclusions and power relations of
established discourses, and [engaging in [security practices]{.mark}
which seek to more directly respond to the insecurity faced by ordinary
people, they [invite an **ethical response to security**]{.mark} and
insecurity]{.underline}. [However]{.underline}, [it limits our
engagement]{.underline} with practices of resistance [if we only see
them as exploring 'better' or 'more ethical' ways of providing
security]{.underline}. [[The **more**]{.mark} **[radical
challenge]{.mark}**]{.underline} to the politics of security
[[comes]{.underline}]{.mark} when we see activism [[not simply as
refusing **particular orders** of security, but]{.mark} as [resisting
the **very**]{.mark} **conceptual and political [foundations of
security]{.mark}**]{.underline}. This final section explores such an
interpretation, looking at the ways in which the most substantive way to
engage the relationships between ethics, security, resistance and
activism comes when we view practices of resistance as (at their best)
working to deconstruct security. I begin by outlining some of the
arguments which suggest that the concept of **[[security cannot]{.mark}
so easily [be refashioned]{.mark} in a more ethical form]{.underline}**
and that thinking in terms of resistance might take us further. I then
look at how we might view such a resistance in the context of political
activism, looking at some examples from anarchist activist groups.¶ A
number of writers have argued that the concept of [security is built
around a series of]{.underline} images, [codes and logics which render
it deeply problematic and a dangerous candidate for
rehabilitation]{.underline}. They have pointed out the ways in which
[our contemporary fascination with]{.underline} proliferating images of
[threat, danger and response]{.underline}, grounded in desperate but
impossible fantasies of control and mastery, [tends towards]{.underline}
authoritarian political formations and [the de facto legitimacy of
dominant power relations]{.underline} (Edkins 2003; Campbell 1998:
Shepherd 2008: 72--75). The pursuit of [[security serves to]{.mark}
**[contain subjects within the existing order]{.mark}**]{.underline},
promising protection in return for some level of compliance or obedience
in a manner not dissimilar to a protection racket (Spike Peterson 1992:
50--52). As Mark Neocleous notes, [[such dynamics]{.mark} serve to
**[neutralise radical]{.mark} political [action]{.mark}**]{.underline},
'encouraging us to surrender ourselves to the state in a thoroughly
conservative fashion' (2008: 4).¶ To understand how the pursuit of
security intertwines with political authority, it is important to
recognise the dependent relationship between security and insecurity.
[Institutions and technologies of [security can only function in a
context of insecurities]{.mark}]{.underline}, which they may identify
and seek to pacify, but which they also need (and for which, of course,
they are often responsible). In Michael Dillon's terms, 'it is only
because it is contoured by insecurity, and because in its turn it also
insecures, that security can secure' (1996: 127). The nature and content
of security depends on its particular relationship with insecurity, with
its exclusions and violences and particular (political) designations of
threat. This regulative binary of security/insecurity intersects with
others that have similar effects, such as order/chaos, inside/outside
and sovereignty/anarchy. All of them regulate politics in a manner which
cements the place of political authority. On the latter dichotomy,
Richard Ashley's comments are pertinent:¶ On the one hand, the sign of
'sovereignty' betokens a rational identity: a homogeneous and continuous
presence that is hierarchically ordered, that has a unique centre of
decision presiding over a coherent 'self', and that is demarcated from,
and in opposition to, an external domain of difference and change that
resists assimilation to its identical being. On the other hand, the sign
of 'anarchy' betokens this residual external domain: an aleatory domain
characterised by difference and discontinuity, contingency and
ambiguity, that can be known only for its lack of the coherent truth and
meaning expressed by a sovereign presence. 'Anarchy' signifies a
problematic domain yet to be brought under the controlling influence of
a sovereign centre ... whether it be an individual actor, a group, a
class, or a political community.¶ (1988: 230)¶ As he identifies the
conservatising regulation at the heart of the sovereignty/anarchy
dichotomy, so would I suggest that a similar process is at work in the
logic of security, privileging that which is rationally bounded,
coherent and compliant, and necessitating the pacification or
pathologisation of that which is not.¶ [Political imaginaries rooted in
binary concepts limit our ethical landscape in a variety of
ways]{.underline}. As V. Spike Peterson argues:¶ \[a\]s long as we
remain locked in dichotomies, [we cannot accurately understand and are
less likely to transform social relations]{.underline}: not only do
oppositional constructions distort the contextual complexity of social
reality, [they set limits on the questions we ask and the alternatives
we consider]{.underline}. True to their "origin" (Athenian objectivist
metaphysics), the dichotomies most naturalized in Western world views
(abstract-concrete, reason-emotion, mind-body, culture-nature,
public-private) are both medium and outcome of objectification
practices. Retaining them keeps us locked in to their
objectifying-reifying-lens on our world(s) and who we are.¶ (1992: 54)¶
In such a context, **[[rather than]{.mark} seeking to [rehabilitate
security]{.mark}]{.underline}** [([and remain within
this]{.underline}]{.mark} [security/insecurity
[dichotomy]{.mark}]{.underline}[), [it might be more productive
to]{.underline}]{.mark} **[resist, displace or [deconstruct
it]{.mark}]{.underline}**.¶ This is not a simple prospect; [refusing the
social fantasy of security would]{.underline}, in Jenny Edkins' terms,
involve 'facing, on a day-to-day basis, questions many of us prefer to
forget, if we can', and 'would involve a shift away from the notion of
sovereign state and sovereign individual ... would [entail the
development of a new vision of political community]{.underline}, one
that was not based on the coming together of discrete participles to
produce closed systems' (2003: 368--369). While [the violent politics of
security is]{.underline} enacted through social institutions, it is also
(as the discussion above shows) [embedded in categories of
thought]{.underline}. The binaries of security/insecurity, order/chaos,
sovereignty/anarchy and more impose a theoretical domination which
conditions political possibility in particular authoritarian ways. As
such, the task of resistance might be to break down such binaries. [This
may take place through]{.underline} mocking, subverting or outwardly
refusing the closures such binaries enact (Rossdale forthcoming-a;
Rossdale forthcoming-b), or through [embracing the proliferation of
definitions of security as a]{.underline}n aporetic [space in which 'to
think and create **new social, ethical and economic relationships**
outside the]{.underline} oppressive structures of [political and
epistemological order']{.underline} (Burke 2007: 30--31).¶ What I want
to suggest here is that we can interpret many practices of activism and
resistance as engaging in precisely this kind of resistance to
security/insecurity; that is, not just as affirming 'more ethical'
securities (though they may also do this), but as mounting a challenge
to the conceptual and political order of security more generally. In a
sense, this is not surprising, so often is resistance framed as that
insecurity, chaos and anarchy which necessitates securing, ordering and
sovereign gestures. It is also an unstable series of interventions,
liable to recuperation within a set of security discourses which swiftly
reposition challenge as threat. Nonetheless, these
[[resistances]{.underline}]{.mark} hold [[open spaces for an]{.mark}
[ethical critique not only of **particular orders of security**]{.mark},
[but]{.mark} more generally [of **the ways**]{.mark} **in which
[security orders]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

### Link\-\--China

#### The aff's description of conflict with China is a [self-fulfilling prophecy]{.underline}\-\--it reinforces violent power relations and causes militarized policies aimed at countering China

Linus **Hagstrom 14**, Senior Research Fellow and East Asia Program
Director at the Swedish Institute of International Affairs, with Bjorn
Jerden, East Asia Programme @ Swedish Institute of International
Affairs, "East Asia's Power Shift: The Flaws and Hazards of the Debate
and How to Avoid Them", Asian Perspective 38 (2014), 337--362, ProQuest

Once we acknowledge that [academic concepts and analyses can have
**constitutive effects**]{.underline}, we need to analyze their
performative role. This means addressing the "double hermeneutic"---the
way "'findings' of the social sciences very often enter constitutively
into the world they describe" (Giddens 1987, 20)---and reflecting on
what our interpretations might "do" (Guzzini 2007, 23). It is not
necessary to accept social constructivism to recognize that [the
practice of [social science is **interlinked with power
relations**]{.mark} outside of academia]{.underline}. Far from scholarly
navel-gazing, [[a reflexive attitude is]{.mark} arguably a
[necessary]{.mark} ingredient of any rigorous social science
practice]{.underline} (Bourdieu and Wacquant 1992).¶ Hence, since [[the
power-shift discourse]{.underline}]{.mark} **[[is not simply a
descriptive account]{.mark} of an independent reality
[but]{.mark}]{.underline}** [[is]{.mark} destined to be **[constitutive
of]{.mark} that [reality]{.mark}**]{.underline}, it is essential to
explore its power. Thomas Diez (2005) makes a similar investigation into
the power of the concept of "normative power Europe" (NPE). He finds
that NPE constructs a benevolent and peaceful identity for Europe, but
also that its alleged peacefulness is contrasted with and therefore
dependent on an idea of what Europe is not---an outside consisting of
"challenging threats" (2005, 636). The double hermeneutic twist is that
the coining of the originally academic NPE concept has become an
important instance of Europe's "normative power."¶ Diez (2013), however,
seems to take for granted the distinction between post-Westphalian
normative powers, which engage in a struggle over the definition of
"normality" in world politics, and Westphalian great powers, which
engage in war, empire building, and physical conquest. "Great powers,"
however, are also the products of normative, discursive pressures, and
they reproduce ideas of what is "normal" great-power behavior. Much of
[[the power-shift discourse reproduces the]{.mark} same familiar
**[self/ other dichotomy]{.mark}**]{.underline} as the NPE literature.
Here, [[the other is a rising and]{.mark} potentially [threatening
China,]{.mark} while the self is a weak but inherently righteous
U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates[/West/Japan]{.underline}.¶ Pan
argues that the "[[China threat discourse" is]{.mark} "always
intrinsically [linked to how US policymakers]{.mark}/mainstream China
specialists [see themselves]{.mark}]{.underline} [([as]{.mark}
representatives of [the indispensable]{.mark}, security-conscious
[nation]{.mark}]{.underline}, for example)" (2004, 306). Hagström
demonstrates how [the [discourse]{.mark} on Japan's "abnormality" and
"weakness" [reproduces a **standard**]{.mark} **of
"[normality]{.mark}**" in world politics that is [centered on the
**ability to go to war**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Postwar Japan is socially
constructed as deviant from this norm and thus as a threat to itself.
This discourse, which emerges in both Japanese and Western academia and
in Japanese political debate, legitimizes a road map for a more
"realistic," "active," "responsible," and "normal" Japan (Hagström
2014).¶ [The power of the power-shift discourse does not stop at the
production of identities and standards]{.underline}. The point is that
[identities and standards produce effects]{.underline}. Pan illustrates
this by showing how the Taiwan Strait Crisis in 1995--1996 was often
understood as "further objective proof of the long-suspected 'China
threat,'" but few acknowledged that "the 'China threat' discourse itself
had played a constitutive role in the lead-up to that crisis" (2004,
323, 320). Moreover, standards in Japan's "normalization" discourse have
enabled Realpolitik changes in its foreign security policy. Chinese
vigilance vis-à-vis Japan and the United States might also be
interpreted as discursive phenomena---the theme of national
reinvigoration is a product of the lingering notion that China has been
continually humiliated and victimized by the great powers since the
nineteenth century. As these examples illustrate, [[the ultimate
power]{.mark} of discourse [is the production of **self-fulfilling
prophecies**]{.mark}]{.underline}. When self-fulfilling prophecies occur
on both sides of a dyad, [[we might end up with a **socially generated
security dilemma**]{.underline}]{.mark} (Johnston 2004).¶ Much research
has warned of self-fulfilling prophecies in the East Asian context. Some
observers suggest that US or Japanese discourses about China might
produce policies of containment, Japanese "normalization" and
"remilitarization," and US "rebalancing" toward East Asia (Pan 2004;
2012; Hagström 2012; Turner 2014a; 2014b). Nonetheless, Japan and the
United States have not yet embarked on containing China. Instead, both
countries have on the whole accommodated China's rise (Christensen 2006;
Jerdén and Hagström 2012). In a similar manner, perceived increases in
bellicose or nationalistic representations in Chinese discourses led
some observers to jump to the mistaken conclusion that this had already
resulted in a more assertive Chinese foreign policy (Jerdén 2014). As
important as it is to remain vigilant against the emergence of socially
constructed security dilemmas, we need to bear in mind that there is no
deterministic connection between discourse and policy---just an enabling
one. Dominant discourses create propensities for action but do not make
any action inevitable. More empirically informed theorizing is needed to
address the questions of how, when, and why discourses make some actions
politically conceivable, easy to communicate, and sometimes even
coercive (Holland 2013).¶ Reflecting on the possible power exercised
through the alternative approaches introduced in this special issue is
also necessary. In this article [we raise analytical and normative
concerns about the ideas of a more "powerful" and "threatening" China, a
"weak" Japan, and a "declining" United States]{.underline}. We argue
that [such [representations risk **offsetting balancing policies** and a
**security dilemma in East Asia**, in line with]{.mark} [the
self-fulfilling prophecy]{.mark} logic discussed above]{.underline}.
These arguments clearly belong to another site of discursive power
production. That we have not seen any obvious balancing policies in East
Asia thus far might arguably be interpreted as one of its effects.

### Link\-\--Hybrid War\-\--Anxiety Geopolitics

#### "Hybrid Warfare" Link\-\--it perpetuates Orientalism that divides the East and West, and turns the case by replicating geopolitical anxiety. 

Jakub **Eberle and** Jan **Daniel 22**, Eberle is senior researcher at
the Institute of International Relations Prague in the Czech Republic
and Daniel is researcher and the Head of the Centre for the Study of
Global Regions at the Institute of International Relations, 01/01/2022,
"Anxiety Geopolitics: Hybrid Warfare, Civilisational Geopolitics, and
the Janus-Faced Politics of Anxiety," Political Geography, vol. 92, p.
102502,
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0962629821001621

1\. Introduction

[This article theorises the relation]{.underline}ship [between
**geopolitics** and **anxiety** and]{.underline} uses this conceptual
argument to analyse and [**critique** the discourse of '**hybrid
warfare**']{.underline}. Conceptual works on the link between
geopolitics and anxiety are scarce, despite contemporaneous appearance
of the claims that we are experiencing (yet another) 'return of
geopolitics' (Auer, 2015; Guzzini, 2012, 2016) and that 'we live in
anxious times' (Eklundh, Zevnik, & Guittet, 2017, p. 1; Kinnvall &
Mitzen, 2020; Zevnik, 2017b) in academic and public debates.
[We]{.underline} fill this gap by [introduc]{.underline}ing the notion
of [**anxiety geopolitics** as a type of **affective discourse**
that]{.underline} emerges to [relieve]{.underline} the [subjects
from]{.underline} the uprooting experience of [anxiety by
anchoring]{.underline} both [the **self** and the]{.underline} alleged
[source of **danger** within a **familiar 'mental map'**]{.underline}.
However, [as anxiety can never be **fully**]{.underline} resolved or
[**repressed**, **discursive fixes**, including **geopolitical** ones,
are **always temporary** and **eventually failing**]{.underline}. We
contend that [anxiety geopolitics is an]{.underline} ultimately
[ambivalent, **Janus-faced** way of 'writing global space']{.underline}
(Ó Tuathail, 1996), [in which anxiety is]{.underline} both
[**repressed** and **reproduced** through **geopolitical
discourses**]{.underline}.

In making these arguments, we build upon and contribute to multiple
literatures in political geography and international relations. First,
conceptualising geopolitics as a discourse through which global politics
becomes spatialised, we borrow from the canon of critical geopolitics.
We follow especially the more recent turn towards affective geopolitics,
understanding affects as intertwined in the process of discursive
construction. So far, anxiety has received only rare attention in this
body of work (an exception is Gökarıksel & Secor, 2020), a gap this
article addresses by theorising the role of anxiety in geopolitics in
detail. Second, in adopting [the]{.underline} argument that
[**construction** of **identity narratives** is a way to **manage
anxiety** and make sense of the world]{.underline}, we engage with the
IR literature on ontological security. Yet, conversely,
[this]{.underline} body of work has [dealt with geopolitics only
**scarcely**]{.underline} (Browning, 2018; Guzzini, 2012, 2016), and
these rare studies rely on a reductionist notion of anxiety. For these
purposes, [we reach to]{.underline} a third literature, namely the work
on [the political dimension of **anxiety** embedded]{.underline}
(mostly) [in **Lacan**ian social theory]{.underline}.

[We]{.underline} then [utilise these]{.underline} theoretical [arguments
to]{.underline} analyse and [**critique 'hybrid warfare'**
(HW)]{.underline}, one of the key security discourses of our time. To
start with, [it is not easy to find out what **HW** is supposed to
**mean**]{.underline} exactly[, as there are]{.underline} many [varying
definitions of]{.underline} the interchangeably used [notions of
'**hybrid wars**', '**hybrid threats**' or '**hybrid
campaigns**']{.underline} (for a magisterial conceptual discussion see
Fridman, 2018). [Most]{.underline} of them typically [label
some]{.underline} form of [combination of 'military and non-military as
well as covert and overt means' of **conflict**]{.underline} (NATO,
2019). This is usually followed by long lists of disparate examples.
According to one EU definition, [**hybrid threats** 'range from
**cyberattacks** on]{.underline} critical information systems, through
the disruption of critical services such as **[energy]{.underline}**
supplies [or **financial services**, to]{.underline} the
[undermining]{.underline} of public [trust in
**government**]{.underline} institutions [or]{.underline} the
[deepening]{.underline} of [**social divisions**']{.underline} (
European Union, 2018). In this logic, HW targets a wide range of
pre-existing social issues, political cleavages and security
vulnerabilities, while seeking to amplify and exploit them for the
benefit of the attacker. The covert and psychological character of such
form of attack then appears to be particularly worrying, as '[hybrid
campaigns]{.underline}' [are]{.underline} 'designed to be [difficult to
**detect** or **attribute**]{.underline}' so as [to '**create
confusion**']{.underline} (European Union, 2018) [and]{.underline}
'[**sow doubt** in]{.underline} the minds of **[target
populations']{.underline}** (NATO, 2019). Clearly, [such definitions of
HW are **so broad** that they]{.underline} can [encompass **almost
anything**]{.underline}; 'any possible combination of military and
non-military means' or even 'any possible hostile activity' as has been
argued elsewhere (Fridman, 2018, p. 155). Consequently and in line with
approaches rooted in critical geopolitics, we do not provide an
authoritative definition of our own. Instead, [we focus on how **HW** is
used in **discourse** to produce certain **(geo)political
effects**]{.underline}.

Apart from being a highly relevant problem, **[HW]{.underline}** also
[presents]{.underline} a particularly fitting case to be analysed as [an
instance of **anxiety geopolitics**]{.underline}. First,
[the]{.underline} close [link]{.underline} between [**HW** and
**anxiety** has been]{.underline} explicitly [recognised by Maria
**Mälksoo**, who shows how HW triggers '**anxiety** about]{.underline}
the [difficulties of **concretising unknown** and **indeterminate
threats**']{.underline} (Mälksoo, 2018, p. 378). Second, the relation
between HW and geopolitics is at the same time strongly pronounced and
marked by a profound tension. On the one hand, [HW]{.underline}
ostensibly [departs from a '**modern'**]{.underline} geopolitical
[imagination based on **territorial states** and
**borders**]{.underline} (Ó Tuathail, 1998). After all, [it is about
information operations]{.underline} in deterritorialised spaces of the
globalised public sphere[, unattributable cyberattacks]{.underline}
carried out by non-state groups, [or]{.underline} the incitement of
[horizontal **protest** movements]{.underline} discontent with the
present political order. On the other hand, [this]{.underline}
'postmodern' [geopolitical imagination]{.underline} (ibid.) [has
been]{.underline} very often [**subdued** to the]{.underline} much more
traditional and ['modern' vision of a '**new Cold War**' between
**Russia** and '**the West**']{.underline} (for critical analyses of
this discourse see Browning, 2018; Ciută & Klinke, 2010; Toal, 2017).
Thereby, [HW has been **incorporated** into the **civilisational
mode**]{.underline} of geopolitical thinking [that divides the world
along an **East**/**West axis**]{.underline}.

Approaching HW as a case of anxiety geopolitics allows us to analyse
this tension and unpack the underlying puzzle: [Why is it
that]{.underline} the ostensibly novel, networked and deterritorialised
phenomena associated with [hybrid warfare get so **easily submerged**
under such an **old**, **static**]{.underline} and territorial
[**geopolitical imagination**?]{.underline} We argue that [the allure of
**East**/**West geopolitics** lies in its **familiarity**, as it
provides a]{.underline} well-worn [**map** that promises to '**make
sense**' of]{.underline} the [anxiety-inducing]{.underline}
manifestations of **['hybrid warfare']{.underline}**. Yet, [we **also
show** that these attempts]{.underline} eventually [**fail**
and]{.underline} the [HW discourse ends up **perpetuating**]{.underline}
the [**insecurities** and **anxieties**]{.underline}, which it was
supposed to resolve. As such, [it serves]{.underline} well to ['a
'**hybrid-industrial complex**' of government **agencies**,
**think-tanks**,]{.underline} non-governmental organizations
(**[NGOs]{.underline}**) [and **pundits'**]{.underline} that emerged
around it (Galeotti, 2019, p. 11). By pointing out to the role of
anxiety geopolitics in this economy of power/knowledge that emerged
around HW, this article provides both an analysis and a political
intervention.

#### "Hybrid War" Link\-\--it's discourse is [Orientalist]{.underline} and [unproductive]{.underline}. 

Jakub **Eberle and** Jan **Daniel 22**, Eberle is senior researcher at
the Institute of International Relations Prague in the Czech Republic
and Daniel is researcher and the Head of the Centre for the Study of
Global Regions at the Institute of International Relations, 01/01/2022,
"Anxiety Geopolitics: Hybrid Warfare, Civilisational Geopolitics, and
the Janus-Faced Politics of Anxiety," Political Geography, vol. 92, p.
102502,
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0962629821001621

Bringing the argument together, [by]{.underline} the notion of [anxiety
geopolitics we conceptualise the **politics of**]{.underline} dealing
with [**anxiety** by linking it to objects]{.underline} and issues
[spatialised through]{.underline} the means of **[geopolitical
imaginations]{.underline}**. By references to geographical categories,
such as states, regions, continents or civilisations, [this]{.underline}
type of [**affective geopolitics** promises to transform]{.underline}
the **[amorphous]{.underline}** and ambiguous **[anxiety]{.underline}**
stemming from range of different issues [into **tangible**]{.underline}
and manageable [**objects** of **fear** taking the form of
**geopolitical threats**]{.underline}. Yet, [as]{.underline} all [such
narratives]{.underline} and images [are **ultimately bound** to fail to
resolve]{.underline} the underlying [**anxiety**, the]{.underline}
analytical [focus must]{.underline} be [extend]{.underline}ed also [to
how]{.underline} these failures are accounted for and how [anxiety
is]{.underline} being further [**reproduced** with]{.underline} the help
of **[geopolitical imaginations]{.underline}**. Therefore, [**anxiety
geopolitics** is]{.underline} ultimately [about]{.underline} the
[oscillation between **repressing anxiety**]{.underline} through the
geopoliticisation of both the 'threat' and the self, [as much as it
is]{.underline} about [dealing with]{.underline} the recurring
[**failures** to **secure**]{.underline} the **[subjects]{.underline}**
produced by such geopoliticisation[, which result in **reproduction** of
**social anxiety**]{.underline}.

3\. Hybrid warfare as anxiety geopolitics: learning from the Czech case

[We]{.underline} will now further develop our conceptual argument by
[discuss]{.underline}ing [hybrid warfare as a **case** of **anxiety
geopolitics**]{.underline}. Some have already shown that [HW plays an
**ontological security role** at the level of **NATO**]{.underline} and
EU, [as it 'links]{.underline} the [uncertainty]{.underline} emanating
[from]{.underline} the **[hybrid]{.underline}** nature of the new
[**threats** to the]{.underline} known and [**routine relationship**
with its]{.underline} traditional [antagonist', that is
**Russia**]{.underline} (**[Mälksoo]{.underline}**, 2018, p. 380).
Following this argument, [Russia\'s aggression]{.underline} suddenly
[gave meaning to]{.underline} a range of hitherto unconnected, yet
omnipresent, and, therefore, [anxiety-inducing problems]{.underline}
that **[NATO]{.underline}**, EU and their [**member states** were
**facing**. **Polarisation** and the rise of **populism**,]{.underline}
widespread [**distrust** in institutions, **erosion** of **international
norms**, **cyberattacks**, **propaganda** and **disinformation**
campaigns]{.underline} suddenly [all '**made sense**', if they could
be]{.underline} discursively [linked to **Moscow** as instances of
'**Russian hybrid warfare'**. HW]{.underline} thus [provided
NATO]{.underline} and EU, [with]{.underline} the elusive ['security' of
knowing one\'s enemy]{.underline} (also Browning, 2018).

Similarly, in Czechia, the [HW]{.underline} discourse [arrived at a time
of]{.underline} intense **[social anxieties]{.underline}** emerging from
a range of different cultural, economic or geopolitical factors. For
many in Czechia (and Central Europe more broadly), [post-Cold
War]{.underline} sense of [ontological security had been provided
through a]{.underline} civilisational [**geopolitical imagination** of
'**the West**']{.underline}, which Central Europe supposedly (re)joined
after 1989 (Cadier, 2019; Kuus, 2007; Todorova, 2009). Best captured by
the slogan of a 'return to Europe', [the vision of embracing **liberal
democracy**, **capitalism** and **Euro-Atlantic political structures**
was]{.underline} seen as [a **panacea** that]{.underline} would [resolve
**all** big **(geo)political questions** once and for all]{.underline}.
However, [these]{.underline} very principles and
[structures]{.underline} arguably [started **crumbling**]{.underline}
precisely [at the time]{.underline} that the [**Central Europeans**
were]{.underline} finally [admitted in]{.underline} EU and [**NATO**.
The **Iraq War** laid bare]{.underline} the [rifts within the **West**,
calling in question its]{.underline} further [relevance as
a]{.underline} coherent **[geopolitical entity]{.underline}** (Browning
& Lehti, 2010; Jackson, 2006). [Resurgent Russia]{.underline} challenged
Western hegemony in Europe and [led]{.underline} 'an influential part of
the Czech political and intellectual [elite]{.underline}
\[[to]{.underline}\] [succumb]{.underline}\[...\] [to
anxiety]{.underline}' (Drulák, 2012, p. 79). [Central European countries
were hit]{.underline} particularly [hard by]{.underline} the [economic
crisis and]{.underline} further [hurt themselves by the]{.underline}
(foreign- or self-imposed) '[Western' medicine of austerity]{.underline}
(Tooze, 2018, pp. 220--238). In the Czech case, [this led
to]{.underline} one of the longest periods of economic recovery in
Europe, accompanied by [a free-fall in public trust]{.underline} in
institutions, with only one in ten Czechs expressing trust in the
government (11%) and the Chamber of Deputies (10%) at the lowest point
in 2012/2013 (CVVM, 2013). Add to this the rise of illiberalism at home
and increasing divides between the 'old' and 'new' EU member states,
laid bare especially during the so called 'refugee crisis' of
2015--2016. As a consequence, [you end up with a region]{.underline}
that is [not]{.underline} so [sure about]{.underline} its feeling about
and towards ['**the West**' any longer]{.underline} (Kazharski, 2018;
Krastev & Holmes, 2019).

Put differently, by mid-2010s, the Czech [society was]{.underline}
already [in a state of **anxiety** and **uncertainty** about
its]{.underline} own [**geopolitical identity** and in search for
**ontological fixes**]{.underline}.3 [This is where]{.underline} the
[**HW discourse** clicks in following Russia\'s aggression against
**Ukraine** in **2014**]{.underline}. To the liberal voices that
dominate security debates, **[hybrid warfare]{.underline}** could be
used to [put a name on]{.underline} this **[anxiety]{.underline}** and
thereby attempt to [suppress it by the construction of familiar
**geopolitical narratives**. By invoking the **prism of a conflict**
between a **liberal**]{.underline}-democratic [**West** and]{.underline}
an [**authoritarian Russia**, all]{.underline} sorts of [problems --
spread of **misinformation**,]{.underline} return of [**nationalism**,
**technological transformations** or **global power
shifts**]{.underline} -- [could be reduced to the logic of
**civilisational confrontation**]{.underline}. This is the conventional
ontological security part of the story, to which we add another step by
showing how this promise of security is never actually fulfilled. [Due
to the]{.underline} supposed [**invisibility** and **omnipresence** of
**hybrid threats**]{.underline} and the insecure position the Czechs
occupy in their own East/West geopolitical imagination, [there is a
**surplus** of **anxiety** that cannot be]{.underline} successfully
**[managed]{.underline}**.

The case study is structured according to the two 'faces' of the
Janus-faced politics of anxiety. [First,]{.underline} we show how
[**hybrid warfare** was used as a sense-making device]{.underline}
promising [to manage **anxiety** by providing a **conceptual link**
between]{.underline} a broad range of [**social issues** and anchoring
them within the familiar **East/West geopolitical imagination**.
This]{.underline} linking could simultaneously make hybrid warfare
meaningful by geopoliticising it, and [reinforce the crumbling East/West
geopolitical imagination]{.underline} by [showing its renewed relevance
in]{.underline} facing [**hybrid warfare**. Second]{.underline}, we show
how the fleeting sense of [ontological security]{.underline} achieved
via this articulation [gets **undone** by]{.underline} the
[recurring]{.underline} re-emergence of [surplus anxiety]{.underline}
and how [HW ends up **reproducing** and **perpetuating**]{.underline}
the sense of insecurity and [**anxiety** it was supposed to **resolve**.
The **analysis** is grounded in]{.underline} a range of **[empirical
materials]{.underline}** from 2014 to 2020, including official
documents, media articles, popular books and parliamentary debates. Two
caveats are in order. First, both Czech government and civil society
have responded to the perceived threat of HW in many ways, including
creating new official institutions and informal initiatives tailored to
deal with it (Daniel & Eberle, 2018). In this article, we focus on the
discourse that made these responses possible. Second, as shown elsewhere
(Daniel & Eberle, 2021), the Czech HW discourse is not monolithic and
includes different understandings of what exactly constitutes a threat
to whom, ranging from military operations targeting public
infrastructures all the way to individual citizen\'s struggles with
media literacy. In this article, though, we downplay these differences
and instead focus on the commonalities that tie these different notions
together as parts of ostensibly the same problem of Russia\'s 'hybrid
warfare'.

3.1. Repressing anxiety: making sense of hybrid warfare through
East/West geopolitics

Starting with the repression side of **[anxiety
geopolitics]{.underline}**, this [can be observed in]{.underline} the
[successive steps through which **ontological security** is sought by
associating]{.underline} a range of [issues with **hybrid warfare**
and]{.underline} then [pinning **HW** onto the **familiar map** of
**East/West geopolitical imagination**. This consists of
four]{.underline} interrelated discursive [moves: identifying
the]{.underline} supposed '**[origin']{.underline}** [of]{.underline}
societal [unease, putting a **name** of the problem, **localising**
it]{.underline} in space[, and endowing it with]{.underline} broader
**[geopolitical meanings]{.underline}**. In this particular case,
[**Russia\'s invasion** of **Ukraine** in **2014** serves as
the]{.underline} supposed [original moment and '**cause'** of anxiety,
**hybrid warfare** is the **name**]{.underline} presented [to make sense
of what is going on, **Russia** is]{.underline} constructed as [the
**threat**, and **civilisational East/West geopolitics**]{.underline} is
used to [give the situation broader **meaning**]{.underline}. Let us now
discuss each of these steps in turn.

[**First**, a **certain event** needs to be constructed as]{.underline}
a supposed point of [origin of]{.underline} the perceived [unease, a
'**crisis'** that dislocates]{.underline} the [symbolic
order]{.underline} (Nabers, 2015). This is the first move in the
repression of anxiety, one in which an 'actually existing' empirical
event is discursively presented as the apparent cause of the deeper
ontological crisis that is affectively experienced as anxiety. In the HW
discourse in Czechia, [Russia\'s aggression against **Ukraine** of
**2014** is presented as such]{.underline} profoundly shocking and
dislocating event. As the otherwise rather down-to-earth and
matter-of-fact military intellectual, Karel Řehka, puts it: 'the Russian
Federation shocked the whole world. The unimaginable was broken into.'
(Řehka, 2017, p. 199) 'Shock' and 'helplessness' are words used also by
a high-ranking Czech diplomat (personal interview, Prague, August 4,
2020). A leading Czech think-tanker then recalls how 'surprised' he was
by Russia\'s invasion and how 'disorganised and fragmented' the security
debates were in the months that followed (Janda, 2017). The affective
experience is captured well also in a widely cited and circulated
popular book, which vividly describes how 'we are walking on the edge of
a cliff' and '\[u\]ncertainty is the only thing that you can count on
these days' (Alvarová, 2017, p. 20). Therefore, [2014 was]{.underline}
constructed as [a **radical breakthrough** into a]{.underline} much
[more **insecure world**]{.underline}. Yet, this move of [identifying
the]{.underline} supposed [**origin** of]{.underline} these dizzying
feelings of [anxiety in **one**]{.underline} particular [**event**
was]{.underline} at the same time already the first step of [seeking
**ontological security** by making sense of it.]{.underline}

[A **second step** is putting the **name** on the
**problem**]{.underline}. It is only the performative and affect-laden
performance of **[naming]{.underline}** that [connects disparate
phenomena]{.underline} together [and creates a **discursive 'object'**
that can be]{.underline} then [dealt with]{.underline} politically
(Laclau, 2005). In our case, [this leads to]{.underline} the creative
appropriation of the [concepts of '**hybrid warfare**' and '**hybrid
threats**', which were]{.underline} virtually [non-existent
in]{.underline} the Czech public [discourse prior to 2014]{.underline}
(Daniel & Eberle, 2018), [and using them as a **linchpin** that
connects]{.underline} all sorts of **[societal problems]{.underline}**.
For an authoritative Czech security document, 'hybrid threat' is a 'way
to wage a confrontation or a conflict', one that is characterised by an
extraordinarily broad spectrum of measures: 'a wide, complex, adaptable,
and integrated combination of conventional and unconventional means,
overt and covert activities, characterised primarily by coercion and
subversion' (Ministry of the Interior of the Czech Republic, 2016, p.
127). The range of actors that can execute such methods is similarly
broad, including 'military, paramilitary, and various civilian actors'
(ibid.). Similarly, an overview of a 'Russian hybrid strategy' provided
by the counterintelligence service includes 'interpretation of modern
history', different ways of 'information warfare',
'networking/infiltration' across the fields of politics, economy, crime,
espionage, culture and education, and military/guerrilla operations
alike (Security Information Service, 2018, p. 7). In statements like
these, [hybrid warfare is **stretched**]{.underline} so as [to
incorporate]{.underline} almost [anything that can be understood
as]{.underline} a **[hostile]{.underline}** activity. A such, [it
becomes a]{.underline}n [**universal object** of **fear**]{.underline},
broad enough [to be]{.underline} used as [a **placeholder** for
all]{.underline} sorts of [anxieties]{.underline}.

In the **[third]{.underline}** move[, hybrid warfare is
**territorialised** by pointing to **Russia** as its]{.underline}
ultimate [source. The '**postmodern**' geopolitical imagination of
**hybrid warfare** in]{.underline} terms of [**flows**, **clouds** and
**infrastructures**, is backgrounded in favour of the '**modern**'
territorial **East/West geopolitics** of a **Russian
threat**]{.underline}. In certain cases, this means little more than
merely pointing out that [it is]{.underline} indeed [**Russia**
that]{.underline} 'has [executed **hybrid operations**]{.underline} \[
...\], [including]{.underline} targeted [disinformation]{.underline}
activities [and **cyber-attacks**]{.underline}', as the Defence Strategy
(Ministry of Defence of the Czech Republic, 2017, p. 7) puts it
explicitly and other documents hint at implicitly (e.g. Ministry of
Foreign Affairs of the Czech Republic, 2015). More interesting are the
[instances]{.underline} which [present Russia not only as **one source**
of threats, but as an **orchestrator** coordinating all]{.underline}
possible [**means** and **actors**]{.underline}. In such cases,
[the]{.underline} 'postmodern' geopolitical [imagination of **networks**
is used]{.underline} and reproduced, yet [with a]{.underline} key
[**twist**: such networks are]{.underline} seen as [hierarchical, with
centre in **Moscow**]{.underline}. In this logic, different [domestic
actors]{.underline} -- 'alternative news' websites [spreading
**anti-Western** narratives, right-wing populists]{.underline}, even
contrarian intellectuals -- [can]{.underline} ultimately [be tied
to]{.underline} an overall [Russia\'s **masterplan**]{.underline} (for
an example see Janda & Kundra, 2016). The counterintelligence agency
reports are an example of this 'puppet-master' approach. One of them
lists 'covert infiltration of Czech media and the Internet' and
'foundation of puppet organizations, covert and open support of populist
or extremist subjects' among the key activities of Russia\'s
'information operations' (Security Information Service, 2016, p. 9).
Another outlines this logic in colourful detail, claiming that 'Russia
is creating a structure in Europe drawing on the concept of the
Comintern (the Communist International; the Third International) founded
by the Soviet Union' (Security Information Service, 2015, p. 11), an
organised network of all sorts of actors 'with pro-Russian stances or
fighting against the system' (ibid., 12).

However, different [actors can be labelled]{.underline} as
[agents]{.underline} or instrument [of **HW** and]{.underline} then
[**geopoliticised** and linked to Russia]{.underline} not only by direct
association, but also [because they are]{.underline} merely [voicing
**opinions** that can be]{.underline} somehow qualified as
'**[pro-Russian]{.underline}**'. As a more recent counterintelligence
report puts it, actually an 'overwhelming majority of disinformation
websites in Czech are the work of Czech \[...\] citizens, who are not
supported by Russian entities.' (Security Information Service, 2018, p.
8) Nevertheless, [this]{.underline} still [makes them a **part** of
a]{.underline} broader [**geopolitical threat**, as 'these
people]{.underline} and their internet projects [are **misused** by
Russia to spread **propaganda** or support]{.underline} other components
of the **[hybrid strategy.]{.underline}**' (ibid., 8) It is [this
imagination]{.underline} of a Russian-orchestrated networked threat that
enables a leading Czech journalist to blankly dismiss the prominent
disinformation website, Aeronet. cz, as 'writing for Putin' (Kundra,
2016b), without any evidence of links to the Russian state known at that
point and with his own subsequent investigations showing that this is
most likely not the case. It also [makes it possible to deal with
**anxieties** by externalising the problem]{.underline}, such as when a
popular book argues that 'Furious hate \[...\] is not Czech, it is
something new, foreign. It came from the outside and 'somehow' entered
into us.' (Alvarová, 2017, p. 88).

In the **[fourth]{.underline}** and final move[, this '**hybrid**'
struggle with Russia is endowed with **meaning** by being inserted
into]{.underline} the whole symbolic structure of [**East/West
civilisational geopolitics**, in which]{.underline} '\[i\]ssues of
[security and geopolitics are]{.underline} \[...\] [reframed in cultural
terms]{.underline}. They become simultaneously geographical, cultural
and strategic concepts, and they diffuse into ever more spheres of
political life.' (Kuus, 2007, p. x) [The]{.underline} societal
[anxieties that are **managed** via]{.underline} the narrative of
[**hybrid warfare** emanating from **Russia**, are]{.underline} now also
made [part of an **eternal struggle** between the **East** and the
**West**. This reactivates]{.underline} the '**[mental
maps]{.underline}**', [in which 'the West' functions as a]{.underline}
desired point of identification and a [promise of **security** and
**prosperity**, whereas 'the East' is]{.underline} seen as ['an
**abyss**']{.underline}, a notion 'which in the Czech political
discourse refers less to a geographical space than to ontological
categories defining the alienated past of the Czech Republic.' (Cadier,
2019, p. 84) As one member of parliament, Jan Bartošek, put it, the
Czechs are left with an unequivocal choice: 'either we will be part of
NATO as a firm ally of our pro-Western orientation, or we will be just
one of Russia\'s many colonies. There is no third way.' (in Chamber of
Deputies, 2018a).

In statements like these, [Russia is presented as]{.underline} a
**[fundamentally different]{.underline}** entity[,]{.underline} a
[quintessentially **Oriental**]{.underline} actor, [belonging to a
**different 'universe'** that]{.underline} 'until nowadays [has not
met]{.underline}' [with the European one]{.underline} (Alvarová, 2017,
p. 70). [Echoing classical **Orientalist tropes**, Russians]{.underline}
supposedly [rely on]{.underline} '\[m\]ysticism,
**[irrationality]{.underline}**, associational [instead of **logical
thinking**]{.underline} -- thus, a model of thinking that is of
different civilisation, the one we know rather from the Orient'
(Alvarová, 2017, p. 193). [The]{.underline} potentially [**catastrophic
consequences** of allying with Russia are]{.underline} then often
[presented through]{.underline} references to [**the past**,
reinforcing]{.underline} the notion [that what is at stake in **HW**
is]{.underline} in fact yet another instance of [a **historical
struggle** between **civilisations**]{.underline}. 'Many of us probably
know our modern history, from 1945 through 1948, Russian advisers, death
of \[foreign minister\] Jan Masaryk, occupation in 1968. The Russian
influence, which simply broke us away from the West, ripped us from
\[our\] democratic development, and has incalculable economic
consequences stretching to this day.' (Helena Langšádlová in Chamber of
Deputies, 2018b).

This idea [that issues like **propaganda**, **misinformation**
or]{.underline} rise of [**populism** are instances of a]{.underline}
dramatic **[geopolitical confrontation]{.underline}**, [is]{.underline}
finally [driven home]{.underline} also [by the notion]{.underline} that
[it is the **West**]{.underline} as a whole [that is under attack, not
just an]{.underline}y [individual **country**]{.underline}. According to
a counterintelligence report, 'the goal of the Russian hybrid campaign'
is 'primarily to weaken NATO and the EU internally, e.g. by weakening
individual member states' (Security Information Service, 2018, p. 7).
Therefore, should the Czechs fail to defend themselves, they are
supposedly endangering something much bigger. According to a member of
parliament, Jan Lipavský, what the Russians want is 'to break European
unity' (Chamber of Deputies, 2018b). [Such]{.underline} ideas [connect
**hybrid warfare** squarely to]{.underline} the [**Messianistic
undertones** of the **East/West geopolitical imagination**, in
which]{.underline} the [Central Europeans serve as **guardians**
defending the **West** at its limit]{.underline}. To paraphrase Milan
Kundera\'s (1984) foundational essay on Central European civilisational
geopolitics, by [fighting hybrid warfare]{.underline}, the Czechs are
[risk]{.underline}ing '[dying for]{.underline} Czechia and [the
West]{.underline}', [which]{.underline} indeed [gives a sense
of]{.underline} deep [**purpose** and **meaning**]{.underline} to their
cause [and produces a]{.underline} strong [anxiety-repressing
narrative]{.underline}.

To sum up this part, one important aspect of the [anxiety geopolitics of
**hybrid warfare**]{.underline} lies in the way how it allows
[channel]{.underline}ling [deep]{.underline}er [anxieties]{.underline}
by [using the **East/West** **geopolitical imagination** to produce
**familiar storylines**, **identities** and **objects** of
**fear**.]{.underline} Despite being constructed as multifaceted, broad
and difficult to detect, [hybrid warfare is made **legible** by being
projected on a]{.underline} familiar '**[mental map]{.underline}**'[,
where it becomes]{.underline} merely [the]{.underline} most [recent
instance of]{.underline} long-lasting [**struggle** between the **West**
and]{.underline} a fundamentally different, antagonist
**[Russia]{.underline}**. However, [the relationship
between]{.underline} the discourses of [**hybrid warfare** and
**East/West geopolitics** goes **both ways**: HW]{.underline} is not
only territorialised by, but also **[gives]{.underline}** broader
[**meaning** to and]{.underline}, in a way, [**promises** to
**reinforce**]{.underline} the [**East/West civilisational
geopolitics**. It is]{.underline} in and [through HW that Russia is
constructed as **fundamentally different** and the **West** presented as
a **coherent entity** under **attack**, as well as something worth
**defending**]{.underline} against the potentially tragic alternatives.
[Through]{.underline} the discourse of [**hybrid warfare**, doubts
about]{.underline} the relevance or [resilience of a **West-centric
geopolitical order** are]{.underline} seemingly [**brushed away**
