and]{.underline} old [**geopolitical identities** are **hardened**. This
is]{.underline} precisely [the logic of **ontological security** that
makes the]{.underline} ideas of a '[**return** of
**geopolitics**]{.underline}' [and the '**new Cold War**' so
**appealing**]{.underline}, as shown by Guzzini (2012, 2016) and
Browning (2018).

3.2. Reproducing anxiety: danger is everywhere and the East is already
within

The [HW]{.underline} discourse arguably [succeeds on]{.underline} the
level of [**meaning**, that is in '**making sense**' of]{.underline} the
[new threats by geopoliticising them in **East/West
terms**]{.underline}. In contrast, [its success is]{.underline} only
**[fleeting]{.underline}** at best [as an **anxiety-repressing
ontological fix**]{.underline}. In this section, [we focus on the
**other face** of **anxiety geopolitics**, showing how]{.underline} the
**[HW]{.underline}** discourse also [contributes to the **reproduction**
of **anxiety**]{.underline}. The argument that security discourses end
up perpetuating the very insecurity they promise to deal with is not new
(e.g. Campbell, 1998). More recently, Heath-Kelly (2018) and Jacobsen
(2020) have advanced it from an explicitly Lacanian perspective and
shown how the discourses of terrorism and cybersecurity are inherently
bound to disappoint the underlying desire for security. The endurance of
these discourses is made possible by the way how they pre-emptively
incorporate an explanation for their own failure. [**Terrorism** and
**cyberthreats** are constructed in]{.underline} such [a **broad**,
**complex** and **omnipresent manner** that no **individual
achievement** can lead to the **resolution** of the
**problems**]{.underline} presented by these discourses. [There
are]{.underline} always [**new risks** and **vulnerabilities**, as the
destruction]{.underline} or neutralisation [of no]{.underline} actually
[existing objects of fear]{.underline} (e.g. Osama bin Laden)
[can]{.underline} ultimately [satisfy]{.underline} the [underlying
**ontological anxieties**. There is]{.underline} always [something
**else** to **worry about**]{.underline}.

Building on Heath-Kelly and Jacobsen, our final argument is that [hybrid
warfare]{.underline} works exactly like their examples, as HW, too, [is
a **discourse** that is]{.underline} simultaneously
[anxiety-**repressing** and anxiety-**reproducing**]{.underline}. In
fact, [we argue]{.underline} that the [linking]{.underline} of [HW and
**East/West geopolitics** produces a]{.underline} particularly [strongly
**anxiety-ridden discourse**. While hybrid warfare presents threats as
**invisible** and **omnipresent** due to their **covert** and
**networked nature**, the **East/West imagination**]{.underline} further
[raises the **stakes** and **salience** of]{.underline} such [threats by
painting them as parts of a]{.underline} titanic [**civilisational
struggle**. The subjects produced by]{.underline} such [**discourse**
can never **rest**, as what may be going on right behind their noses is
not]{.underline} just **[one]{.underline}** particular
**[cyber-attack]{.underline}**, a single conspiracy theory that has
gained traction on Facebook, or merely one instance of information
stolen by a spy. Instead, [it is a battle for the **future** of '**the
West**]{.underline}' and the Czech belonging to it[, one where the
**alternative**]{.underline} option [is]{.underline} the most
[tragic]{.underline} one[: a descent]{.underline} deep [into the
'**abyss**' of '**the East**']{.underline} (Cadier, 2019).
[We]{.underline} will now [illustrate these arguments by focusing on the
**unfathomability** of **HW**, the **shifting nature** of]{.underline}
the **[threats]{.underline}** it produces[, and]{.underline} the
[anxieties]{.underline} inherent [in]{.underline} the [East/West
geopolitics that underpin it]{.underline}.

[**First**, the '**failure to secure**']{.underline} (Heath-Kelly, 2015)
[is]{.underline} pre-emptively [incorporated in]{.underline} the [HW
discourse by its portrayal of]{.underline} the [**looming dangers** as
**insidious**, **invisible** and]{.underline} even [**impossible** to
detect]{.underline}. As a key security document states, '\[t\]he
principal risk to which a subject attacked by a hybrid campaign is
exposed lies in the fact that they will not be able to identify the
hybrid campaign -- in time, in its full scale, or at all' (Ministry of
the Interior of the Czech Republic, 2016, p. 129). According to the
deputy Helena Langšádlová, [what we are]{.underline} supposedly
[facing]{.underline} are highly dangerous, yet ['**creeping threats**',
which we 'cannot see on a day by day basis]{.underline}' (in Chamber of
Deputies, 2019). This leads some to claim that 'at no point in history
was it so extremely difficult to decide, if we are at war, or not'
(Táborský, 2019, p. 164). While there may be little drama on the
surface, [no one can ever **rest** in **this logic**. An attack may
be]{.underline} already [**underway**, one that can]{.underline} even
[be approximated to **war**]{.underline}, literally [at **any
minute**]{.underline}. As the Special Forces general, Karel Řehka, put
it at a conference organised in the Czech parliament: 'In a way, we are
already at war, we just do not realise it or are not able to admit it.'
(Lang, 2015) [This]{.underline} is a mode of thinking that [produces
highly **anxious subjects**, in a]{.underline} constant [sense
of]{.underline} the '**[expectant dread]{.underline}**' that is anxiety
(Hook, 2015, p. 117), one not yet having a clear referent and directed
towards all possible yet still unknown dangers that may materialise at
any time.

[It is in these notions that an]{.underline} unspecified [**existential
threat** may be hidden behind **mundane events**]{.underline} that the
otherwise backgrounded 'postmodern' geopolitical imagination of HW
suddenly [kicks back in. If the]{.underline} 'modern' [East/West
geopolitical imagination]{.underline} 'made sense' and
[provided]{.underline} at least [some]{.underline} fleeting [ontological
security]{.underline} by pointing to Russia, [this is **frustrated**
by]{.underline} the [**surplus anxiety** produced via this]{.underline}
'postmodern' [imagination of **insidious**, **hidden networks**
operating in **physical** and **cyberspace**]{.underline} alike. In
fact, as the discourse postulates, [we may not]{.underline} even
[know]{.underline} that [it really is Russia]{.underline} in the first
place [that is behind a]{.underline} particular [incident, as hybrid
attackers]{.underline} seek to [create 'an environment where
responsibility]{.underline} for these activities [cannot]{.underline}
(at least formally) [be **attributed**]{.underline} to them, or at least
only speculatively and with great difficulty' (Ministry of the Interior
of the Czech Republic, 2016, p. 127). We may ultimately never know if
this or that mundane event is actually not a part of something much
bigger, as [it is the]{.underline} very [aim of the attacker to 'prevent
a **clear interpretation** of **events**]{.underline} and the discovery
of their interconnectedness' (Ministry of the Interior of the Czech
Republic, 2016, p. 127). Therefore, while the prevalent 'modern'
geopolitical reading of HW enables channelling anxiety by constructing
Russia an object of fear, it still remains far from the manageable fear
of 'fight or flight' (Kinnvall & Mitzen, 2020, p. 241). This is because
[these]{.underline} supposedly [known aspects]{.underline} of the danger
[are]{.underline} constantly being [accompanied]{.underline} and
disrupted [by]{.underline} the [**surplus anxiety** of the **'unknown
unknowns'** stemming from]{.underline} the partial inclusion of [the
**'postmodern' geopolitical reading**. This]{.underline} effectively
[pre-empts the **HW discourse** from]{.underline} ever
[solving]{.underline} the [problems it is]{.underline} supposedly
[designed to **tackle**, as it is]{.underline} wholly [unclear how to
**act** upon **threats** that we are not]{.underline} yet [aware of or
do not know how to **make sense of**]{.underline}.

[**Second**, this oscillation between]{.underline} the known and the
unknown and between [fear and anxiety manifests]{.underline} itself [in
the]{.underline} constantly [shifting construction of what
is]{.underline} supposed [to be the]{.underline} exact [**nature** of
the **threat** coming from **Russia**.]{.underline} This is best
illustrated in the wording of the annual reports of the Czech
counterintelligence agency (SIS), which reinforce the notion that
[Russia is]{.underline} indeed [a **severe threat**, yet the **precise
character** and **shape** of this threat appears to be
changing]{.underline} year after year. As already mentioned, in first
annual public report reflecting on the situation after Russia\'s
aggression against Ukraine, the SIS warned that their assessment showed
the formation of what was spectacularly labelled as the 'New
Reincarnation of the Comintern' -- a loose network of allied actors,
similar to the Cold War Soviet-controlled network of ideologically
affiliated political parties, agitators and agents. The danger was
portrayed as a new version of this tried and tested strategy, which
Russia supposedly employs to connect different groups dissatisfied with
the Western liberal democracy (Security Information Service, 2015, pp.
11--12).

However, the spectre of the Comintern was a one-off, never to appear
again. Instead, SIS later concluded that the threat resided in a much
looser combination of often uncoordinated actions of Russian
intelligence, authentic Czech individuals not in any way linked to
Moscow but 'only' spreading their own 'pro-Russian' worldviews, and
finally, even in the lack of education about modern history in schools
(Security Information Service, 2018, pp. 6--8). This trend of diffusing
the danger from a Kremlin-coordinated network to seeing the threats in
mere ideological resonances further continued. The most recent report
notes that there is a 'transition from state-controlled or directed
activities to spontaneous actions' of like-minded actors. 'When Russian
state officials express what they desire to happen (for instance by
spreading manipulative information), proxy actors without any links to
the Russian state proceed to action on their own initiative and based on
what they think the officials might want' (Security Information Service,
2020, p. 9).

Through this move, [the spectre of '**Russian hybrid warfare**'
can]{.underline} now [encompass virtually **anything** that can
be]{.underline} somehow [interpreted as matching with the]{.underline}
desires or interests of [the Kremlin, without]{.underline} any need for
[proving **direct links**, as these are no longer]{.underline}
considered [necessary. The **construction** of '**a threat**' now
includes]{.underline} even [situations when 'a foreign power does not
engage in]{.underline} any [**direct action** and keeps its
**distance**]{.underline}, while using various ways (PR, instigating
statements, propaganda etc.) to inspire individual persons to take
action' (Security Information Service, 2020, p. 9). Put differently,
Russian threat can be present even where there is no direct Russian hand
whatsoever. Literally any individual with views somehow similar to those
of the Russian state can be seen as part of it, which makes the idea
that security can ever be achieved virtually impossible. Instead, [this
logic contributes to]{.underline} the reproduction of [an **anxious
society**, defined by 'the constant presence of]{.underline} the
possibility of that [threat, and]{.underline} with it, [the
sense]{.underline} that [government cannot]{.underline} fully
[protect]{.underline} the [people]{.underline} and that danger resides
in the everyday' (Kinnvall & Mitzen, 2020, p. 247).

Third, this notion that the possibility that something 'Russian' may be
insidiously present deep within the Czech society then revives also the
old anxieties inherent in the 'modern' East/West geopolitical
imagination itself. Central Europeans\' self-positioning in East/West
geopolitics is highly ambivalent (Kuus, 2007; Todorova, 2009). On the
one hand, being or becoming part of 'the Western civilisation' is a
source of ontological security. On the other hand, this security is
never quite complete or permanent. This is because Central Europeans
locate themselves at the very limit of the West, as 'European edge-men'
(Mälksoo, 2010, p. 5), whose membership in the civilisation must
constantly be proved as it can always be taken away -- especially by the
forces of the 'East'. The 'old shadow of Yalta', the feeling that the
region\'s 'freedom could yet again be expendable in times of crisis' is
constantly present (Mälksoo, 2010, p. 75). Put differently, East/West
geopolitics equips the Czech 'pro-Western' security intellectuals with a
'mental map, where the country is depicted as being on the "edge" of
Europe and constantly risking to "fall" into an abyss \[...\]
traditionally characterised as the "East"' (Cadier, 2019, p. 84). While
the East/West geopolitical imagination can succeed in spatialising the
threat in the Russian 'other', the position it grants to the self is
always potentially insecure. Therefore, it is also East/West geopolitics
itself that produces subjects that are constantly on alert, facing the
constant 'possibility of loss of one\'s soul' (Balaska, 2019, p. 8) that
defines the experience of anxiety.

Such [fragile **geopolitical self-positioning**]{.underline} further
[fuels the search for **new** and **new sources** of **hybrid
threats**]{.underline}, as failing to uncover and face them may have
existential consequences, especially given that 'Easternness' may have
already infiltrated and compromised the Czech social body. This notion
of 'East within' links to the above discussed unfathomability and
invisibility of HW and manifests itself in multiple ways. For one
counterintelligence report, it takes the form of smuggled ideas and
narratives, presented 'in a way leading Czech citizens to believe they
are recipients of opinions held by fellow citizens not of Russian
propaganda' (Security Information Service, 2015, p. 11). Similarly, a
popular book on 'fake news' geopoliticises social attitudes en bloc by
identifying them along East/West axis, presenting the 'disappointed'
part of society as 'seeing a model in Russia, or perhaps China' (Gregor,
Mlejnková, & Zvolsi.info, 2018, p. 62). For others, the 'East within'
takes the form in the physical presence of 'Putin\'s agents' who
supposedly 'quite likely teach your children at universities, you meet
them for a coffee in your favourite café or work in normal jobs.'
(Kundra, 2016a, p. 88).

[The **anxiety geopolitics** of **hybrid warfare** is]{.underline} thus
also [about **creating** the]{.underline} normatively [highly disturbing
'"**red** under **every bed**" mentality']{.underline} (Fridman, 2018,
p. 3) [and applying 'the ethics of **total war**]{.underline} \[...\]
[even to the **smallest skirmish**']{.underline} (Galeotti, 2019, p. 8).
Therefore, [viewing **security threats** through]{.underline} the prism
of [**hybrid warfare** reproduces a **highly anxious society** and
perpetuates]{.underline} the [justification for those '**intellectuals**
of **statecraft**']{.underline} (Ó Tuathail & Agnew, 1992) [that would
not hesitate to provide]{.underline} the sort of **[geopolitical
fixes]{.underline}** that were discussed in the previous sections.
Thereby, [the discourse ends up **reproducing itself**, as the **two
faces** of **anxiety geopolitics**]{.underline} not only disrupt, but
also [dialectically reinforce one another: geopolitical discourse
emerges to **repress anxiety** and provide **ontological security**, yet
the anxiety inherent in the discourse **disrupts ontological security**
and]{.underline}, to come full circle, [creates the need for
**geopolitical discourses**]{.underline}. The result is a society
oscillating between its desire to avoid anxiety and the repeated
frustration thereof; an affective pulsation that has arguably been
elevated to a dominant mode of politics of (in)security in our present
time (Eklundh et al., 2017), of which [hybrid warfare is a **prime
example**]{.underline}. As similar patterns of anxious over-reaction and
securitisation of broad areas of social life have been recognised by
authors writing about HW in different empirical contexts (Fridman, 2018;
Galeotti, 2019; Mälksoo, 2018; Ördén, 2019), we believe that the problem
with 'hybrid warfare' is of a more general nature and the relevance of
our analysis reaches beyond the Czech case.

4\. Conclusion

Introducing anxiety geopolitics as a conceptual linchpin between
disparate arguments from debates on critical geopolitics, ontological
security, and politics of anxiety, this article made two central
contributions. First, we have theorised the relationship between
geopolitics and anxiety, moving beyond the existing accounts above all
by pointing out the Janus-faced character of anxiety geopolitics, in
which anxiety is both repressed and reproduced. Second, using the case
of Czechia, we have argued that hybrid warfare is a discourse that
constantly oscillates between repressing anxiety by geopoliticising the
source of danger in East/West terms, and subverting its own
constructions by presenting the threats as insidious, invisible, and
constantly shifting. Therefore, [we contend]{.underline} that the [HW
discourse is **structured** in a way that cannot achieve its **purported
ambition** to secure populations against '**hybrid threats**'
and]{.underline} instead [ends up **producing more insecurity** and
**anxiety**.]{.underline}

[Our argument has clear **normative implications** that
expand]{.underline} the existing [criticisms of **HW** by
putting]{.underline} the [underlying **civilisational geopolitics** in
**spotlight**]{.underline}. This should help us challenge the
technology-centred presentism of the HW discourse, [showing]{.underline}
that [many]{.underline} of the [ostensibly **unprecedented** concerns
are]{.underline} in fact [**reheated** versions of **narratives** that
date back]{.underline} decades if not **[centuries]{.underline}**. More
importantly, [it enables us to point to]{.underline} the presence of
some of the [**highly problematic aspects** of **East/West thinking**,
especially those that have been]{.underline} rightly [criticised as
**Orientalist**, **chauvinist** or]{.underline} even
**[racist]{.underline}** (see e.g. Said, 1978; Todorova, 2009).
[Realising]{.underline} the presence of [civilisational geopolitics
makes it possible to ask whether]{.underline} certain [portrayals of
**Russia** in **HW debates**]{.underline} -- e.g. [as **barbaric**,
**irrational**, **irredeemable**]{.underline} -- [may not represent
**narcissistic projections** of the **'Western' self**, rather than
**credible threat assessments**]{.underline} (Chernobrov, 2019).
Importantly, [these are not **scholastic matters** of **concern** just
for **ivory-tower peaceniks**]{.underline}, as the proponents of HW
sometimes like to put it. Instead, [these criticisms have **clear
implications** for **security policy**]{.underline}. As recognised even
among NATO\'s own analysts and officials (Caliskan & Liégeois, 2020;
Rühle, 2019), [adopting the prism of **HW** and labelling Russia as
an]{.underline} inherently [**irrational enemy** is detrimental
to]{.underline} leading [**a productive debate** on]{.underline} the
exact nature of [the challenges]{.underline} that [Putin\'s regime
**poses**]{.underline} [and **formulating**]{.underline} appropriate and
[**efficient strategies** to respond to it]{.underline}. We add to it by
highlighting that the [HW discourse not only '**undermines strategic
thinking**']{.underline} (Caliskan & Liégeois, 2020), [but]{.underline}
also [never]{.underline} actually [manages to provide **security** and
creates]{.underline} highly concerning societal side-effects
([perpetuation of **anxiety**, proliferation of **Orientalist
images**]{.underline}).

There are also important qualifications of our argument and avenues for
further research. First of all, we certainly do not claim that everyone
who ever spoke of 'hybrid threats' or 'hybrid warfare' would find
themselves in agreement with the examples used in our analysis, or be
instantly guilty of Orientalism. While the article focused on
overarching themes, there is certainly variation within the HW
discourse, not only in Czechia (Daniel & Eberle, 2021), but also in
Germany, UK or EU (Janičatová & Mlejnková, 2021; Monsees, 2020; Ördén,
2019). One intriguing possibility for further research is in showing the
variation in the way how geopolitical thought is invoked and anxiety
repressed and reproduced within the different versions of the HW
discourse. More generally, the theoretical argument should also be tried
and developed in other contexts.

### Link\-\--Hybrid War\-\--Anxiety Inevitable

#### Anxiety is [inevitable]{.underline}. It is [impossible]{.underline} maintain a [stable]{.underline}, [coherent identity]{.underline}. That [turns case]{.underline}\-\--ontological insecurity drives [ineffectual policy fixes]{.underline}. 

Jakub **Eberle and** Jan **Daniel 22**, Eberle is senior researcher at
the Institute of International Relations Prague in the Czech Republic
and Daniel is researcher and the Head of the Centre for the Study of
Global Regions at the Institute of International Relations, 01/01/2022,
"Anxiety Geopolitics: Hybrid Warfare, Civilisational Geopolitics, and
the Janus-Faced Politics of Anxiety," Political Geography, vol. 92, p.
102502,
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0962629821001621

Both [Guzzini]{.underline} and Browning [are **critical** of using
**geopolitics** as a tool of **anxiety management**, citing]{.underline}
its [adverse effects]{.underline} both [in **normative
terms**]{.underline} (creation of enemy-images, building an exclusionary
'Fortress Europe'), [and in]{.underline} the [consequential decrease in
**physical security**]{.underline} (e.g. [as a result of]{.underline}
escalating [tensions between **antagonists** locked]{.underline}-in
[in]{.underline} their **[hardened identities]{.underline}**). [However,
both]{.underline} also appear to [ground their argument in]{.underline}
the [assumption that **anxiety**]{.underline} ultimately [can be
**managed**]{.underline} (more or less) successfully, one that is shared
by the Giddensian mainstream of ontological security studies. While
scholars making such arguments would consider the price of
anxiety-management via geopolitics as too steep, they still accept that
such a trade-off can be made in the first place and that one\'s
ontological security can in fact be gained at the expense of physical
security or the well-being of others. As some of the most recent debates
in ontological security scholarship recognise (Browning, 2019; Cash,
2020; Gustafsson & Krickel-Choi, 2020; Kinnvall & Mitzen, 2020;
Rumelili, 2020), these issues stem from Giddens\' problematic
conceptualisation of anxiety that is adopted by most ontological
security work in critical geopolitics and IR. [To sharpen]{.underline}
the [analysis of]{.underline} the role of [**anxiety** in
**geopolitics** and deepen the **critique**]{.underline} made by Guzzini
and Browning[, an **additional** theoretical **step** is
**needed**.]{.underline}

For this purpose, [we reach to]{.underline} the [literature
on]{.underline} the politics of [anxiety drawing]{.underline} mainly
[on]{.underline} the work of Jacques **[Lacan]{.underline}**, a
psychoanalyst whose ideas have been increasingly prolific in IR (for an
authoritative overview see Zevnik & Mandelbaum, 2021), and, to a lesser
extent, critical geopolitics (Klinke, 2016; Laketa, 2019; Müller, 2013).
Our approach shares the Giddensian notion that [anxiety is an
inarticulable]{.underline}, traumatic and potentially paralysing [affect
that subjects]{.underline} attempt to [**avoid** and **repress**.
**However**,]{.underline} instead of seeing it as merely something that
'lurks' behind as the dark other of normal life, [anxiety
is]{.underline} understood as [a **durable affective condition**
interwoven in **everyday experiences** as well as **social processes**
and **political institutions**]{.underline}. It doesn\'t just lurk, [it
is **always already there**, as it is **present**]{.underline} in the
key mechanisms that hold society together: in [the production of
**social subjects** and]{.underline} the creation of [**rules** through
which these are **regulated** and **bound together**]{.underline}.

In this reading, [anxiety is correlative to the]{.underline} very
[**emergence** and **existence** of the **subject**]{.underline} as
such. [It is 'a]{.underline} type of **[expectant dread']{.underline}**
or 'a crushing experience of 'out of placeness'' [that arises 'when the
**subject**, unable to ground themselves in]{.underline} either
[a]{.underline} functional [horizon of **values** or a **reliable
social** or **subjective identification**, fears]{.underline} that [they
might be]{.underline} somehow [**swallowed up, devoured**']{.underline}
(Hook, 2015, pp. 117, 119). [It is less about]{.underline} the
disorientation and [uncertainty caused by **one**]{.underline}
particular **[crisis]{.underline}** (e.g. Russia invading Ukraine,
Covid-19 arriving) [and more about]{.underline} being reminded of
[the]{.underline} ultimately [**irreducible fragility** of **all
things**, including **human lives** and **social orders**]{.underline}.
In this sense, [anxiety is]{.underline} an affect [linked to
experiencing the limit of one\'s]{.underline} own [existence as a
**subject**, something that signifies the **encounter** with '**the
real**']{.underline}, to use the Lacanian term for the internal limit of
social order and/or the biological limit of human existence. [Anxiety
can **never be**]{.underline} fully [**managed** or **repressed**, as it
is a reaction to]{.underline} being confronted with what is for Lacan
[the]{.underline} ultimate [**reality** of]{.underline} human
[**existence**: the]{.underline} fundamental **['groundlessness of
meaning']{.underline}** (Balaska, 2019, p. 25) [and]{.underline},
therefore, [the **impossibility** of]{.underline} ever [achieving a
**coherent** and **stable identity**]{.underline}. In this sense,
anxiety is the affect that 'does not deceive' (Lacan, 2014, p. 160), as
it does not cover over the void at the heart of every social identity,
but rather confronts us with it at the level of bodily sensation. To put
it differently, [subjects are **always**]{.underline} ultimately
[**ontologically insecure**, as **no narratives**]{.underline}, routines
or relationships -- [**personal** or **geopolitical**]{.underline} --
[can ever]{.underline} fully [deal with the]{.underline} omnipresent
[possibility that **things** may not **make sense** and everything can
**break down any minute**. Anxiety emerges as]{.underline} the affect
that signifies 'the need for the **[stabilisation]{.underline}** of the
subject' (Burgess, 2017, p. 29), [yet this **stabilisation** is **doomed
to fail**]{.underline} in the longer term.

Therefore, in contrast to readings that see it as an aberration,
[anxiety is **omnipresent**, even though it]{.underline} clearly [varies
in]{.underline} its [**intensity** across **time** and
**space**]{.underline}. Sometimes it is experienced more often and more
strongly, while there may be other, less anxious times (Solomon, 2012
makes this argument with respect to affects in general). [The
current]{.underline} social [condition is]{.underline} arguably [one
when anxiety is]{.underline} on the **[high]{.underline}**; leading some
authors to argue that we are now living in an 'anxious society', 'a
society on constant alert, [despite having no identifiable **existential
threat**]{.underline}. Instead, [there is the]{.underline} constant
[presence of the **possibility** of that **threat**]{.underline}, and
with it, the sense that government cannot fully protect the people and
that danger resides in the everyday' (Kinnvall & Mitzen, 2020, p. 247,
emph. added; Eklundh et al., 2017). [Such]{.underline} (highly) anxious
society [was created by]{.underline} the congruence of [a **range** of
**factors**. Some point to]{.underline} the effects of
[**neoliberalism** and **austerity**]{.underline} that produce the
widespread feelings of powerlessness and loss of control (Hirvonen,
2017). [Others focus on]{.underline} the **[social media]{.underline}**
economy that requires users who are constantly obsessively checking for
news updates, notifications and the affective gratification coming from
'retweets' and 'likes' (Davies, 2019). [For]{.underline} yet
[others,]{.underline} the proliferation of [anxiety is connected
to]{.underline} the societal changes linked to [**gender**, **race** and
**immigration**]{.underline} (Ali & Whitham, 2018; Klinke, 2016; Zevnik,
2017b), [or]{.underline} the [**'security creep'** perpetuated by
discourses]{.underline} and practices [of **counterterrorism** and
**cybersecurity**]{.underline} (Heath-Kelly, 2018; Jacobsen, 2020).

This extended understanding of anxiety as ontologically conditioned and
socially circulated 'nervous states' (Davies, 2019) without a clear and
concrete referent object opens the possibility to think (geo)politics of
anxiety in broader and arguably more critical terms than in conventional
ontological security literature. On the one hand, [the **Lacanian
approach**]{.underline} broadly [**agrees** with the
Giddensian]{.underline} ontological security [framework with respect to
how **subjects**]{.underline} try to [**deal with anxiety**: by
'**pinning**' it onto an **object** and transforming it into
a]{.underline} more [**tangible fear** of **something**]{.underline}. In
this manner, 'fear becomes a way of easing anxiety; of attaching a
signifier (an object of fear) to what is otherwise an unfounded
experience of unease' (Zevnik, 2017a, p. 237). [Such '**politics of
fear'**]{.underline} then [transforms]{.underline} the crushing and
paralysing experience of [anxiety into the management of '**concrete
objects**]{.underline} that [we]{.underline} have
[invented']{.underline} (Hirvonen, 2017, p. 261), [such as]{.underline}
the geopolitically coded [**'Muslim terrorists'** or **'Russians
hackers'**]{.underline}. In this step, [anxiety is]{.underline}
ostensibly [eased]{.underline} or repressed [by]{.underline} the
[production of]{.underline} (geopolitical) [narratives that '**make
sense**' of the]{.underline} unpleasant [experience by giving it a
**name** and placing it on a **map**.]{.underline}2

On the other hand, however, [the Lacanian take allows us to
account]{.underline} also [for]{.underline} the [politics that is
**imminent** in the **failure** of]{.underline} these
**[anxiety-managing attempts]{.underline}**. As we have argued, [all
narratives built around]{.underline} particular objects of [**fear**,
including **geopolitical ones**, are]{.underline} eventually [failing,
as these **objects** are]{.underline} ultimately [**'false targets'**.
They are]{.underline} merely [temporary **discursive vessels**
functioning as **placeholders** for]{.underline} expressing deeper
[**ontological anxiety**, which]{.underline} is bound to **[strike
back]{.underline}**. Consequently, [attempts to make
societies]{.underline} ([feel]{.underline}) more [secure]{.underline},
habitually [end up making them]{.underline} (feel) equally, or even
more, [**insecure** and **anxious**, regardless of]{.underline} how much
[effort]{.underline} is [invested in]{.underline}to [fighting **this**
or **that**]{.underline} particular **[threat]{.underline}**. As
Heath-Kelly (2015, 2018) and Jacobsen (2020) have shown, [successful
**security discourses**]{.underline} like counter-terrorism or
cybersecurity have actually [managed to **internalise** this '**failure
to secure'**]{.underline} (Heath-Kelly, 2015) by incorporating it into
their very structure. According to them, [**counter-terrorism** and
**cybersecurity** are constructed as dangers so **slippery** and
**multifaceted** that once we resolve **one problem**]{.underline} (by
killing Osama bin Laden, resolving a particular cyberthreat),
[a]{.underline} whole [new **range** of]{.underline} terrorist groups or
cyber [issues **emerges**]{.underline} to occupy their place.

Consequently, and in contrast to conventional ontological security
literature, [**politics** of **anxiety**]{.underline} is not only about
managing and repressing it through narratives, routines and
relationships. It [is]{.underline} also [about]{.underline} other ways
of [manipulating anxiety for **political purposes**, including
**reproducing**]{.underline}, nurturing [and **spreading** it via the
**construction** of **threats** so opaque and **widespread** that they
cannot **be possibly resolved**, like **terrorism**, **cybersecurity**
-- or **hybrid warfare**. By its **repeated failure** to **secure**,
such discourses hold societies in the]{.underline} anxious ['state of
**constant** and **heightened alertness'**]{.underline} (Davies, 2019,
p. xii), [making them ready to accept]{.underline} a range of
pre-[**emptive security measures** across **all**]{.underline} possible
**[areas]{.underline}** of social life.

### Link\-\--Hybrid War\-\--Defense Narrative

#### The link [could not]{.underline} be more [explicit]{.underline}\-\--the AFF's [worries]{.underline} about [cyberattacks]{.underline} and [information war]{.underline} are a [narrative]{.underline} that elevates [hybrid war]{.underline} to existential threat. 

Jan **Daniel and** Jakub **Eberle 21**, Eberle is senior researcher at
the Institute of International Relations Prague in the Czech Republic
and Daniel is researcher and the Head of the Centre for the Study of
Global Regions at the Institute of International Relations, 2021,
"Speaking of hybrid warfare: Multiple narratives and differing expertise
in the 'hybrid warfare' debate in Czechia," Cooperation and Conflict,
Vol. 56, Issue 4, pp. 432-453,
<https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/00108367211000799>

The defence narrative: protecting the state

[The first **narrative** pictures **HW** as a **problem** for **national
defence**]{.underline}. In this reading, which is closer to the original
formulations of HW in US military thought (Bahenský, 2018; Fridman,
2018; Hoffman, 2007), '[hybrid']{.underline} [is]{.underline} above all
[a form of **war**, or]{.underline} at least [a]{.underline}n
accompanying [feature of]{.underline} conventional [**military
confrontation**. This leads to a narrative]{.underline} embedded
[in]{.underline} strategic studies and [**IR realism**, where the threat
comes from **power politics** backed up by **military
force**]{.underline} and concerns, primarily, the state and its
institutions. As such, [the threat is]{.underline} to be [minimised by
**building up** and updating]{.underline} national and **[alliance
institutions]{.underline}** and defence capabilities. The key sources
developing this narrative in the archive are the Security Strategy, the
Defence Strategy and publications and interviews of Karel Řehka, a
former Special Forces general and perhaps the most publicly visible
military intellectual in Czechia

[This]{.underline} narrative [portrays HW a]{.underline}s
[a]{.underline}nother [**type** of **war**. It is animated by
the]{.underline} broader [Clausewitzian discourse about **war** as a
phenomenon with **unchanging 'nature'**, but]{.underline} constantly
[shifting '**character'**, where the latter]{.underline} is
[depend]{.underline}ent [upon]{.underline} the social and technological
developments of [a given age]{.underline} (Von Clausewitz, 1989). In
Řehka's words,

wars have always been and always will be a part of human society. And as
long as people have different interests and there are conflicts between
them and there comes a situation, when they are ready to use violence,
we have a war. (Řehka in Golis, 2018b)

What is different, though, is the social and technological environment,
and especially the rise of the information domain as one of the
battlefields (Řehka, 2017: 54).

[The realist]{.underline} and military [logic is **apparent** in the
**portrayal** of the **threat**, which is]{.underline} understood as [a

[are ready to **pursue**]{.underline} their

Therefore, [the Kremlin and]{.underline} its [HW]{.underline} approaches
[are a threat first and foremost because of Russia's **military power**

In this military- and state-centred logic of the defence narrative, [the

Consequently, [the response is centred around]{.underline} reinforcing

As far as the broader knowledge and expertise behind [the defence

and]{.underline} the authority of [the **state apparatus**]{.underline}.
While the official documents rarely elaborate on the sources of their

this respect, Řehka provides lengthy quotes from governmental documents
and reports of intelligence services (Řehka, 2017: 178--182).

[HW is]{.underline} thus [made known through a]{.underline} broad [body
of **academic** and]{.underline} applied [**military knowledge**,

alphabet soup of specialised agencies.

### Link\-\--Hybrid War\-\--Raitasalo

#### "Hybrid Warfare" is a [prime example]{.underline} of [securitization]{.underline}\-\--it [resecuritizes]{.underline} great power relationships and [excuses]{.underline} the shaky foundations of IR. 

Jyri **Raitasalo 17**, Lieutenant Colonel Raitasalo, PhD, is a senior
staff officer at the Finnish Ministry of Defence Planning Unit
(strategic planning) and was head lecturer of strategy at the Finnish
National Defence University, 2017, "Getting a Grip on the So-Called
'Hybrid Warfare'," ASPJ Africa & Francophonie,
https://www.airuniversity.af.edu/Portals/10/ASPJ_French/journals_E/Volume-08_Issue-3/raitasalo_e.pdf

[The outlook on]{.underline} international [security]{.underline} within
the Western security community, and particularly in Europe, has
[changed]{.underline} dramatically [during the **post-Cold War
era**]{.underline}.1 At the same time, [the West has gone
**out-ofarea**, developed an **expeditionary military mind-set** and
fought **several wars**]{.underline} of choice against third-rate
military adversaries [in the name of]{.underline} "military crisis
management," "counterinsurgency warfare," and [the "**War on
Terror**."]{.underline}

During the past 25 years, the shared [Western understandings
on]{.underline} international [security]{.underline} have gone through a
process of foundational change. Western notions of international
security and military affairs [have gone through a **paradigm
change**]{.underline}. At the core of this change has been the belief
that [we have been able to overcome]{.underline} the [Cold
War]{.underline} era [**zero-sum logic** to]{.underline} international
[**security** and **adversarial relations** with]{.underline} other
[great powers]{.underline} of the day[---]{.underline}namely [**Russia**
and **China**]{.underline}. The West has moved away or gradually grown
out from containing and deterring state-based military threats towards
ever broadening notions of international security. [The
"new"]{.underline} post-Cold War-era Western [security perspective
included]{.underline} the stability of the globalizing international
system and **[human security]{.underline}** as perspectives through
which [to analyse]{.underline} security [**threats** and]{.underline}
appropriate **[responses]{.underline}** to these threats.2

[Now that Russia]{.underline} has [used]{.underline} very traditional
[**great-power tools** in **Ukraine**]{.underline} since 2013[,
and]{.underline} also in **[Syria]{.underline}** since 2015, many
[Western states]{.underline} have [found themselves in need of a **"new"
framework**]{.underline}---any framework---[to cope with this **return**
of the **past**]{.underline} in contemporary international politics.
After all, most European states could not conceptualize military threats
in Europe only three years ago. Similarly, the United States has pulled
out its troops from Europe with the conviction that state-based military
threats in Europe are unimaginable. Thus, during the last 25 years, most
Western states have focused on committing military troops to
multinational expeditionary operations with scant direct connections to
Western states' survival or national security interests. After Crimea,
[advocating **hybrid warfare** has been a way to **(re)securitize**
the]{.underline} traditional **[great-power perspective]{.underline}**
on international security[---an approach]{.underline} that [Western
states]{.underline} had [desecuritized since the end of the **Cold
War**]{.underline} as the West was redefining international security on
its own terms.3

[The **hybrid warfare thesis** is represented by the idea]{.underline}
that [Russia]{.underline} has [invented a **new approach** to
**statecraft** and **military affairs** after the war in **Georgia**. It
reflects]{.underline} more than anything the [collective **Western
surprise** that]{.underline} the very traditional actions of
[Russia]{.underline} have [caused]{.underline}. This article argues that
[the **hybrid warfare thesis**]{.underline} has [catered to
the]{.underline} Western [need to explain]{.underline} and understand
[Russia's actions]{.underline} in Ukraine [as]{.underline} the post-Cold
War-era [Western conceptualisations of international
security]{.underline} have [prove]{.underline}d [to be laid on **shaky
foundations**]{.underline}. Great-power rivalries, spheres-of-influence
thinking, propaganda, coercion, the use of proxies, spying, and the use
of military force by great powers did not become extinct with the demise
of the Cold War even though many Western analysts and statesmen thought
they had. Recent actions of Russia have revealed this flaw in the
Western approach to post-Cold War-era international security.

The emergence and development of the hybrid warfare thesis has been
politically useful---highlighting the changing nature and shortcomings
of the postCold War-era Western perspective on international security.
[The analytical **utility** of the **hybrid warfare thesis**
is]{.underline} more [limited. Many]{.underline} of the supposedly new
[elements of]{.underline} the so**[-called hybrid warfare]{.underline}**
and the myriad of associated and supposedly new forms of warfare
[are]{.underline} in fact [**normal practices** of statecraft rather
than **novel expressions** of **war**.]{.underline} Many Western
strategic analysts and statesmen have problems in dealing with these
traditional tools of statecraft due to the development of Western
perspective on international security during the post-Cold War era. More
than anything, the rise of [the **hybrid warfare thesis** is a
collective]{.underline} Western [attempt to **domesticate**
the]{.underline} traditional [threat]{.underline} that [Russia
poses]{.underline} today.

### Link\-\--Hybrid War\-\--Malksoo

#### "Hybrid War" Link\-\--it entrenches [deep securitization]{.underline} and generates a [risk society]{.underline}. 

Maria **Mälksoo 18**, Senior Lecturer in International Security at the
Brussels School of International Studies at the University of Kent,
2018, "Countering hybrid warfare as ontological security management: the
emerging practices of the EU and NATO," European Security, Vol. 27,
Issue 3, pp. 374-392,
<https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/09662839.2018.1497984>

Below, [I]{.underline} set out to [show how]{.underline} the [**hybrid
war discourse** epitomises]{.underline} the contemporary [ontological
insecurities of]{.underline} the EU and [**NATO**. Attempts
to]{.underline} frame bold institutional [responses to]{.underline} the
["hybrid" threats,]{.underline} notably [via]{.underline} the [promotion
of resilience]{.underline} as the institutional equivalent of a sense of
OS, [mark "bringing the **war** back in" for]{.underline} the
international [security management]{.underline} profiles
[of]{.underline} these two [major Western organisations. NATO and the
EU's]{.underline} emerging **[discourse]{.underline}** and practice [in
countering **hybrid warfare** seek to prove their **continuing
relevance** in the contemporary era]{.underline}. The [**preoccupation**
with **hybrid threats**]{.underline} has [made]{.underline} the concept
of [war]{.underline} empirically [more **available**]{.underline} for
the EU. Albeit war continues to be normatively unacceptable, public
discussion of concrete practices to counter various "hybrid" moves from
third parties as part of the broader "hybrid warfare" countering
paradigm is far from a taboo for the EU in this day and age.4
[NATO]{.underline} as a more traditional security organisation [is
wrestling harder with the **threshold of war** becoming]{.underline}
increasingly [fluid in the context of **"hybrid"
engagements**]{.underline}. Lawyers debate whether "hybrid warfare"
calls for updates in the law of armed conflict. Ultimately, [it is the
legal characterisation which determines whether a situation]{.underline}
is considered to [amount to **armed conflict**, and]{.underline}
accordingly, [whether peacetime law or law of war applies]{.underline}
(O'Connell 2015). The hybridisation of warfare further challenges the
(substantively anyway dubious) legal distinction between international
and non-international armed conflicts (Reeves 2016). Meanwhile,
[the]{.underline} general [**acceptance** of the **term "hybrid
war(fare)"**]{.underline} also [signals the **politicisation** of
the]{.underline} established [**legal definition** of
**war**]{.underline} as a particular practice that takes place when
certain specific conditions have been fulfilled[.]{.underline}

Brandishing "war" on political contestations of varying intensity has
furthermore ethical implications (cf. Franke 2015). As the EU and NATO
are grappling with honing their response to the menaces and tactics
combining a mixture of special forces, backdoor proxies, information
campaigns, and "digital warfare" (e.g. Ilves 2016), having further added
the non-state variant of the "hybrid threat" to the mix (such as Daesh),
a broader question transpires about the repercussions of the alleged
hybridisation of warfare and its perpetrators for the study of security
in IR.

The article proceeds in four sections. The first section gauges various
definitions of hybrid warfare, outlines their relation to the notion of
ontological insecurity, and the consequent countering attempts of hybrid
threats to the mirror-image process of OS-seeking. I make a threefold
proposition: [hybrid warfare is disturbing **ontologically**, because it
embodies the entanglement of **politics** and **war**]{.underline} in
the contemporary era[; **epistemologically**, because it unhinges the
**war**/**peace binary**]{.underline} implicitly
[underpinning]{.underline} the [IR]{.underline} discipline (Barkawi
2016);5 [and]{.underline} last but not least [**ethically**, because
the]{.underline} inherent [danger of becoming a **monster** in the
course of fighting **monsters**]{.underline} (aka the efficient
countering of hybrid warfare) [is]{.underline} particularly [poignant
for **democratic polities**]{.underline} (cf. Nietzsche 2003, Aphorism
146). The second section applies various OS-attuned lenses on the
institutional responses of the EU and NATO to hybrid warfare, and the
third section empirically illustrates the argument. The article
concludes with a call for caution: [adopting the "**everything** is
**dangerous**"- approach]{.underline} further [blurs the **fuzzy line**
between **politics** and **war**, adding heat to]{.underline} the calls
to [revisit the international **laws** on **armed
conflicts**]{.underline} (cf. Winter 2011). The justifiability of
illiberal methods in safeguarding liberal values constitutes the crux of
the OS dilemma for the Western security community battling the "hybrid
menace".

Hybrid warfare as the epitome of ontological insecurity

Much of the strategic studies literature has been animated by the
problem of uncertainty in international relations and, by extension, the
consequences of uncertainty for the management of states' security
dilemma (Rathbun 2007, cf. Mitzen and Schweller 2011). [**Ambiguity**
is]{.underline} likewise [the **original sin**]{.underline} the "[hybrid
warfare]{.underline}"-notion [draws on. Yet]{.underline}, the concept
"**[hybrid warfare]{.underline}**" [is **itself faulty** of definitional
ambiguities]{.underline}. Although an increasingly utilised concept in
the contemporary strategic and policy discourse, ["hybrid
warfare"]{.underline} refers to a number of distinct phenomena, and
consequently means different things to different people. It [lacks a
clear and **uniform definition**]{.underline}, hence embodying and
[perpetuating the **countenance** it seeks to capture. "Hybrid warfare"
is]{.underline} thus [victim to its]{.underline} own **[conceptual
plasticity]{.underline}** (Tenenbaum 2015, p. 43).

The heterogeneous origins, composition, and use of the concept can be
somewhat clarified by distinguishing between (i) hybrid threats (as
complex and multidimensional modern menaces, crisscrossing multiple
issue areas and amplifying one another); (ii) hybrid warfare (as a
particular mode of waging war, combining conventional and
unconventional, coercive and non-coercive means, capabilities, tactics
and formations in a centrally organised and orchestrated manner), and
(iii) hybrid war (as "a form of violent conflict that simultaneously
involves state and non-state actors, with the use of conventional and
unconventional means of warfare that are not limited to the battlefield
or a particular physical territory" (Jacobs and Lasconjarias 2015, p. 3,
cf. Jonsson and Seely 2015, Browning 2002, p. 2). Tenenbaum (2015)
offers a useful genealogy of the "hybrid warfare" concept, highlighting
the notion's distinct effects at the political and strategic,
operational, tactical and capability levels. Yet, the practical use of
the term "hybrid" has interchangeably and rather confusingly moved
between these different levels of analysis, seeking to capture the
interconnected nature of modern vulnerabilities, the multiplicity of
stakeholders in the contemporary security game (i.e. state and non-state
actors, regular and irregular forces), along with the diversity and
simultaneity of conventional and unconventional means used, ranging from
military, political, economic, diplomatic, technological to criminal
modes of engagement (Hoffman 2007, Glenn 2009, Pawlak 2015). Writings on
hybrid warfare thus tap into both the literature on
asymmetric/counterinsurgency warfare (McCuen 2008, cf. Winter 2011) and
that on interstate wars (Gerasimov 2013). No wonder that "hybrid" has
come to accommodate as varied phenomena as Russia's takeover of Crimea
in 2014 and its involvement in the ongoing conflict in eastern Ukraine,
Russia's meddling in the elections of various countries around the world
via sophisticated phishing, doxing, and fake news campaigns, and the
regional and global operating logics of politically ambitious non-state
organisations, such as Hezbollah, Boko Haram, Al-Qaeda, or Daesh, or
even criminal structures (e.g. the drug cartels in Mexico; see further
Tenenbaum 2015).

Drawing on the emerging scholarship on hybrid warfare, three core
interconnections can be flagged between hybrid warfare (as an empirical
phenomenon and a discursive trope) and (the study of) OS in IR. First,
along the ontological dimension, "[hybrid warfare]{.underline}"
[captures the]{.underline} increasing [**porousness** of lines between
**politics** and **war**]{.underline} in the contemporary era, thus
[defying]{.underline} the [attempts of **organising life** and social
relations in a **particular way**]{.underline} (cf. Huysmans 1998).
While many critics of the notion maintain that hybrid warfare remains
just another variation on the old Clausewitzian understanding of war as
the continuation of politics with a mixture of other means (cf. Gray
2007), others nonetheless highlight the particularity of the
contemporary hybrid engagements. For the scholars finding distinct added
value in the notion, the calibration and central coordination of the
convergence of the various regular and irregular elements, further
amplified by the new technological vulnerabilities and capabilities in
contemporary hybrid engagements, have made the creeping indeterminacy
about what war exactly is and how to go about it in the present day only
more intense. The envisioning of hybrid engagements as a pre-phase of a
full-scale military attack further adds to the ambiguity between th
boundaries of warfare as essentially organised, reciprocal fighting and
politics as a peaceful space of ordinary goings-about.

For those at the receiving end, [hybrid warfare emerges as an
**epitome** of **ontological insecurity**, referring to the "deep,
incapacitating state of not knowing which dangers to confront
and]{.underline} which to [ignore]{.underline}, i.e. how to get by in
the world" (Mitzen 2006, p. 345). [It is a]{.underline}n urgent
[reminder of the chameleon-like character of war --]{.underline} that
is, [war's tendency to]{.underline} [constantly change]{.underline} its
[forms]{.underline} as well as its appearances (Clausewitz 1976, p. 80).
At the most fundamental level, then, [hybrid warfare epitomises the
**blurry line** between **politics** and **war**. It]{.underline} also
[symbolises the struggle for]{.underline}, and fear over losing,
[control "over]{.underline} the ends, ways and means of [nations,
communities and societies]{.underline}" (Ruiz Palmer 2015, p. 61).
Assuming that collectivities' agency is predicated on identification and
routinisation (Greve 2017, p. 7), [uncertainty about]{.underline} the
nature of [external threats does]{.underline} not just generate physical
insecurity but also [evokes **ontological insecurity** for]{.underline}
the [institutions]{.underline} (such as military) and organisations in
question. [Hybrid warfare exposes collective actors to]{.underline} the
fundamental [existential questions]{.underline} about the continuity of
their external environment as they know it and their own finitude,
[with]{.underline} the [related anxiety about]{.underline} the
difficulties of [concretising]{.underline} unknown and [indeterminate
threats]{.underline} (cf. Ejdus 2017). Thus formulated, [hybrid
warfare]{.underline} directly targets actors' "security of being"
(Kinnvall 2004, p. 746), [disturb]{.underline}ing [the stability
of]{.underline} their [**sense-making** attempts of the **surrounding
world** and]{.underline} the [events they are faced with,]{.underline}
and [threatening to unsettle]{.underline} the established
[institutionalised routines]{.underline}. Instead, [hybrid
warfare]{.underline} itself [emerges as an "**institutionalization of
doubt**", provoking]{.underline} considerable **[ontological
insecurity]{.underline}** (cf. Giddens 1990, pp. 92--94).

[**Epistemologically**,]{.underline} and on a second note, ["hybrid
warfare"]{.underline} thus significantly [unsettles the "**war**/**peace
binary**" which]{.underline} has arguably [structured]{.underline} the
[thinking about]{.underline}, and categorisation of, [war]{.underline}
in the Eurocentric tradition of its study. This particular understanding
of war(time), cleanly juxtaposed to peace(time) has been attuned to the
needs of an international system of sovereign nation-states, enabling
the consequent categorisation of war into international (inter-state)
and civil (intra-state) war (Barkawi 2016). "Hybrid wars" emphatically
challenge a neat war/peace binary, which has, however, served as a core
source of OS for the discipline of IR. There is a family resemblance
between the concepts of "hybrid warfare" and the notion of "unpeace"
introduced by Kello (2017), seeking to capture the ambiguous, yet
persistent irritants by virtual weapons on the international order.

[**Finally**, hybrid warfare meets **OS** at the]{.underline}
intersection of **[ethical security studies]{.underline}** (Browning
2016, Browning and McDonald 2013). Just as OS-seeking practices need to
be analysed with an eye on the ethical conundrums and dilemmas they
might give rise to due to the exercise of power along the way (e.g.
Mälksoo 2015, Rossdale 2015), the "social turn" accompanying the
hybridisation of warfare in various empirical settings, and the
consequent countering attempts of hybrid warfare, require equally intent
ethical attention (see Owens 2012). If hybrid conflicts are understood
as full spectrum wars ... combining a struggle against an armed enemy
and a wider struggle for, control and support of the combat zone's
indigenous population, the support of the home fronts of the intervening
nations, and the support of the international community (McCuen 2008, p.
108), [hybrid warfare]{.underline} really [appears as]{.underline} yet
[another variation on the **theme** of "**war** amongst the **people**".
The intermingling of **political** and **military
activities**]{.underline} thus not only underscores the need for their
parallel examination (Smith 2005), but also [calls for **caution** in
the **lax use** of **war** as a **metaphor**]{.underline} for
engagements of various types and intensity. [If society must be
**defended**]{.underline} (Foucault 2003) [against]{.underline} nearly
[**everything**, at **all times**, and if everyone becomes **connected**
and]{.underline} potentially [**targeted** in the global **"hybrid war"
zone**, what is left of **politics**, and the]{.underline} delicate
[balancing act between **security** and **democratic
liberties**?]{.underline} The implications of constant public perception
management in the service of protecting the freedom of speech in the
"post-truth" world are perhaps not fully fathomable yet. The
disconcerting potential of the increasingly prominent strategic
communication discourse in the EU6 and NATO7 for the everyday of
democratic politics is already evident (cf. Garton Ash 2016). [Securing
a]{.underline} core **[democratic value]{.underline}** -- freedom of
speech/freedom of media -- [via]{.underline} an **[obsessive
emphasis]{.underline}** on proactive and [purposeful communication
management points at the]{.underline} classic [paradox of militant
democracy]{.underline} whereby [the]{.underline} very [attempt to
**defend** democracy might inadvertently damage it]{.underline} (cf.
Müller 2016, p. 253).

Managing hybrid warfare as ontological security-seeking

[Hybrid warfare emerges as the **embodiment** of **uncertainty**
for]{.underline} the EU and [**NATO**. The meanings of **uncertainty**
range from **fear**, **ignorance**, **confusion**, and]{.underline}/or
[**indeterminacy** in **IR theory**]{.underline} (Rathbun 2007, pp.
533--534). It is certainly possible to apply all these lenses and
consequent emphases on reading the EU and NATO responses to the
fundamentally undetermined condition of hybrid warfare, ambivalence par
excellence. Paraphrasing Huysmans's (1998) original application of the
OS concept in IR, [the Western countering attempts of **hybrid warfare**
tell a **security story** wherein a fear of **uncertainty**, or of the
**unknown** trumps a]{.underline} more concrete **[fear of
death]{.underline}** at the hands of other people. [As hybrid
warfare]{.underline} vividly [symbolises "an epistemological fear -- a
fear of not knowing"]{.underline} (Huysmans 1998, p. 235) (e.g. when war
is waged at "us"; what is this "new" kind of "war" really all about
etc.), [the]{.underline} emerging strategic [responses of]{.underline}
these two core [Western organisations demonstrate how the]{.underline}
double [fear of death and not knowing]{.underline} quite when and in
which ways to expect it, [gets objectified]{.underline} in order [to
make the]{.underline} growing [list of]{.underline} potentially
[existentially dangerous subjects and phenomena more
**concrete**]{.underline}, palpable and conceivable for oneself. [Hybrid
warfare]{.underline} thus [emerges as the "**unbearable
void**"]{.underline} (Huysmans 1998, p. 237), [which needs to be
**objectified**]{.underline}, in order [to become "**knowable**"
and]{.underline} (more) **[tolerable]{.underline}**.

Huysmans (1998) defines OS as a strategy for managing the limits of
reflexivity by fixing social relations into a symbolic and institutional
order. In his interpretation, OS thus concerns the general question of
the political, or "how to order social relations while simultaneously
guaranteeing the very activity of ordering itself" (Huysmans 1998, p.
242). [Hybrid warfare]{.underline}, by definition, [destabilises
the]{.underline} traditional [cognitive]{.underline} security
[environment of]{.underline} states and [international organisations,
and]{.underline} consequently, [renders their **identity** insecure.
Crafting]{.underline} the institutional [responses to hybrid
warfare]{.underline} thus [becomes a "**dread management**" exercise
wherein]{.underline} daily [**security administration** attempts,
seeking to **objectify** the]{.underline} abstract [**fear of death**
through constructing **concrete enemies** and]{.underline} thus
[introducing]{.underline} a level of **[certainty]{.underline}**,
nonetheless [remain **toothless** at the inability to "**hierarchize
threats**" in an atmosphere of]{.underline} potentially
[permanent]{.underline} state of [**crisis** and
**urgency**]{.underline} (Huysmans 1998, p. 243). The pursuits of daily
security and OS ("security of being") thus collapse into each other.

Hybrid warfare indicates a multitude of possible contingencies,
generating anxiety about one's ability to remain oneself and to continue
to act. It is thus linked to "anxiety over the vulnerability of
\[Western\] power" (Bell 2012, pp. 230--231), threatening the West about
losing its particularistic form of existence (cf. Creppell 2011, p.
455). Anxiety, in contrast to fear, which per Giddens (1991, p. 43)
constitutes a response to a specific threat, concerns "perceived threats
to the integrity of the security system of the individual" (Giddens
1991, pp. 44--45, cf. Browning and Joenniemi 2017, p. 38; Rumelili
2015). OS-seeking aims to mitigate the effects of such hard uncertainty,
bringing it within bearable limits (Mitzen 2006, p. 346). Routines are
instrumental here, as they "pacify the cognitive environment ...
'inoculating' individuals against paralytic, deep fear of chaos" (Mitzen
2006, p. 347).

[Being able to survive]{.underline} the "[hybrid threats]{.underline}"
[is]{.underline} directly [pertinent to the **survival** of]{.underline}
the EU and [NATO as]{.underline} particular kinds of [organisations,
underpinned]{.underline} and driven [by **specific values** -- which
cannot be **sacrificed** or **diluted** in the struggle for **physical**
and **institutional survival**. Yet, hybrid warfare disturbs the **OS**
of]{.underline} the EU and [**NATO** in]{.underline} subtly [distinct
ways]{.underline}. Due to the particularities of their institutional
set-ups and historical foundations, the OS drives of the two
organisations are somewhat distinctive. [NATO's history is]{.underline}
occasionally [told through its **surpassing** of]{.underline} a sequence
of [crises, albeit the end of the **Cold War** and]{.underline} the
collapse of its original enemy [created a situation of]{.underline}
unprecedented [**uncertainty** and **ontological insecurity** for the
Alliance]{.underline}. Meanwhile, the EU's ontological insecurity is a
more recent phenomenon, stemming mostly from the Eurocrisis and the
looming possibility of a Grexit a few years ago; the refugee/migration
crisis in Europe of 2015 (Dingott Alkhoper 2018; Mitzen 2018); the
actual UK decision on Brexit in 2016 (Browning 2018); and the rise of
populist politics within the EU (Kinnvall, Manners, Mitzen 2018) and the
United States. [NATO]{.underline} as a military alliance [has
been]{.underline} historically more [accustomed to]{.underline} the
**[othering]{.underline}** practices [of]{.underline} concrete
geographical [places]{.underline}, whereas the EU is generally regarded
as an organisation that has risen above geopolitical othering,
juxtaposing itself to Europe's dark past instead (Rumelili 2018, Subotic
2018, and Della Sala 2018). Accordingly, the EU might be more at home
with hybrid threat management due to its historically broader
conceptualisation of security through the paradigm of intertwined risks
rather than being focused more strictly on "the threat, use and control
of military force" in the manner of a traditional defence alliance (Walt
1991, p. 212; Manners 2002). Regardless of its perpetual transformer's
self-image, [hybrid threats represent the uncomfortable
"**hard**"]{.underline} or "fundamental" [**uncertainty** for
NATO]{.underline} (cf. Mitzen 2006, p. 346) [and are]{.underline} thus
[ontologically **disturbing** for the security of its **self** as a
**traditional alliance** with a **collective security pledge** bound to
"**armed attack**"]{.underline} against [one or more of its **member
states**]{.underline}.

In order to achieve OS, actors strive for "routinizing their relations
with significant others" (Mitzen 2006, p. 342). Routines help to keep
ontological fears at bay, out of everyday discursive consciousness
(Mitzen 2006, p. 348). From this perspective, [NATO's **naming** and
**shaming strategy** vis-à-vis Russia as part of its hybrid warfare
countering strategy links the **uncertainty** emanating from the
**hybrid**]{.underline} nature of the new [**threats** to
the]{.underline} known and [**routine relationship** with
its]{.underline} traditional **[antagonist]{.underline}**.8 The
incapacitating difficulties related to planning ahead in anticipating
and countering hybrid engagements are thus somewhat alleviated by the
latter's attachment to a known rival. Russia's hybrid intervention in
Ukraine has concurrently provided NATO with the familiar parameters of
the cognitive "cocoon" (Giddens 1991, pp. 39--40) which enables the
alliance to reproduce its cognitively "knowable" world. A Mitzenian
reading of NATO's emerging response to hybrid threats/warfare thus
points at the renewed routinisation of the "programmed cognitive and
behavioral responses" to Russia as the alliance's original nemesis.

[Restoring]{.underline} traditional [**vigilance** vis-à-vis
**Russia**]{.underline}, buttressing the forward defence along the
Alliance's eastern flank with the respective military reinforcements,
contingency planning and exercises[, the]{.underline} emerging [response
of **NATO** to the **hybrid menace** could]{.underline} also [be read as
a]{.underline}n attempted [restoration of a]{.underline} known
[**normative order** for the **North Atlantic Alliance**. Hybrid warfare
endangers the]{.underline} basic [features of the]{.underline} said
[normative order]{.underline} (or that of the broadly conceived Western
security community in general, including the EU as well) [as
its]{.underline} efficient [countering would]{.underline} likely
[compromise the]{.underline} underlying [principles for this
**order's**]{.underline} particularistic **[existence]{.underline}**
(cf. Creppell 2011, p. 450). As a threat of subversion, **[hybrid
warfare]{.underline}** [exposes the]{.underline} internal
[vulnerabilities in the **body** of]{.underline} the traditional
security alliance (**[NATO]{.underline}**) and a self-proclaimed
post-modern security actor (i.e. the EU; cf. Cooper 2004). [This
concerns]{.underline}, in particular, [the **ever-elusive**]{.underline}
(and contested) **[balance]{.underline}** [between
**national**]{.underline}/organisational **[security]{.underline}** [and
**individual liberties**]{.underline} (cf. Waldron 2003, Neocleous
2007), [as s**trategic communication** is]{.underline} inherently [at
odds with **free speech** as a **core value** of **Western liberalism**.
Countering **hybrid warfare** is conducive of generating a **security
predicament** of **perpetual pre-emption** which]{.underline}, by
definition, [would indicate pre-emptive gathering of **all sorts** of
**data**]{.underline}, thus likely [infringing on]{.underline} the
**[privacy]{.underline}** of the individuals [for]{.underline} the sake
of the organisational/regional/national [security. This]{.underline}
precautionary logic [resonates with the **risk society**]{.underline}
approach [which]{.underline} [characterises]{.underline} the [struggling
with a sheer **volume** of **risks** with]{.underline} potentially
[fundamental consequences as a **key feature** of
**modernity**]{.underline}, implying that "decisions are ... made not in
context of certainty, nor even of available knowledge, but of doubt,
premonition, foreboding, challenge, mistrust, fear, and anxiety" (Ewald
2002, p. 294).

[A less **sympathetic reading** of]{.underline} such an [ontological
"**dread management**"]{.underline} exercise [would regard]{.underline}
particularly [NATO's]{.underline} emerging [narrative]{.underline} and
practice [about **hybrid warfare** as]{.underline} an example of
[turning]{.underline} unknown [anxieties into]{.underline} "the
manageable [certainties of **objects** of **fear**]{.underline} to
physical security [through **securitization**]{.underline}" (Browning
and Joenniemi 2017, p. 38, Rumelili 2015, Steele 2008, p. 64). NATO's
and the EU's "dread management" strategies vis-à-vis the hybrid
"spectre" tune us further in the direction of Croft and
Vaughan-Williams's (2017, p. 27) research agenda, asking specifically
"\[w\]hose dread is managed and at what cost for whom?", thus
[illuminating]{.underline} the [ethical pitfalls related to]{.underline}
their [OSseeking. Due to]{.underline} the [omnipresence]{.underline} and
ambivalence [of **hybrid threats**, NATO]{.underline} and the EU's
[countering strategies of hybrid warfare verge on "**deep
securitization**"]{.underline}, which Abulof (2014, p. 397) defines by
its distinctly high scale and scope of securitising moves. The [hybrid
warfare discourse has become **ubiquitous** in]{.underline} the
respective repertoires of the EU and [**NATO**, with **hybrid
threats**]{.underline} [framed as **imminent**, **protracted**, and
**existentially endangering**. **Securitisation**]{.underline} thus
[emerges as a **response** to]{.underline} the [**ontological
insecurity**, with a promise of "**mitigating**]{.underline} the
[existential angst arising from **death** being]{.underline} both
**[certain]{.underline}** [and undetermined]{.underline}" (Abulof 2014,
p. 403, Huysmans 1998). Both [NATO]{.underline} and the EU [are engaged
in **collective securitisation**]{.underline} [of **hybrid
warfare**]{.underline} since the threat in question has a systemic
referent -- that is, it "imping\[es\] upon international and collective
identities, or the rules and norms governing interstate interactions"
(Sperling and Webber 2017, p. 26). [**Hybrid threat management**
by]{.underline} the EU and [**NATO** could]{.underline} accordingly [be
understood as]{.underline} the [institutionalisation of]{.underline}
their respective organisational [OS-seeking]{.underline} via strategies
of "being" (aimed at constructing a strong narrative to buttress a
continuous and esteemboosting identity) and "doing" (focused on an
attempt to uphold a stable cognitive environment through routinised
practice "whilst also undertaking action contributing to a sense of
integrity and pride") (Flockhart 2016, p. 799). While the EU is
emphatically defending core democratic values (freedom of expression,
freedom of assembly, media freedom and access to information) (European
Parliament 2016), [NATO's countering of **hybrid threats**]{.underline}
[demonstrates]{.underline} more explicitly the
[intertwining]{.underline} of its **[physical survival]{.underline}** as
an efficient political-military alliance [and a sense of
**OS**]{.underline} [as the principal warden of]{.underline} the
**[European security]{.underline}** (and more broadly Western world)
order. [The inability to honour the **collective defence pledge** would
be a blow to NATO's **identity**]{.underline} [and mean its]{.underline}
(almost certain) **[death]{.underline}**. [The security of its
"**body**"]{.underline} (i.e. physical security) [and
"**self**"]{.underline} (i.e. ontological/identity security)
[are]{.underline} therefore [less **distinguishable**]{.underline} than
in the case of the EU which is a far more complex political setting,
with only relatively recent claims (albeit with increasing
assertiveness) in the traditional security sphere.9 Regardless, for both
organisations, [countering **hybrid threats**]{.underline} [serves as a
reactive **self-legitimation**]{.underline} [as they]{.underline} thus
[reassert]{.underline} their [relevance and ability to be of
**assistance**]{.underline} for their respective [member
states]{.underline} and populations.

### Link\-\--Hybrid War\-\--Cyber-Resilience

#### Resilience is an [anxiety management]{.underline} strategy. 

Maria **Mälksoo 18**, Senior Lecturer in International Security at the
Brussels School of International Studies at the University of Kent,
2018, "Countering hybrid warfare as ontological security management: the
emerging practices of the EU and NATO," European Security, Vol. 27,
Issue 3, pp. 374-392,
<https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/09662839.2018.1497984>

Hybrid warfare in daily security management

[A bird's-eye view of]{.underline} the EU and [NATO's]{.underline}
emerging [management strategies of **hybrid warfare** demonstrates how
the]{.underline} looming [hybrid agenda is]{.underline} directly
[tapping into]{.underline} the [everyday security concerns of
these]{.underline} two main [Western]{.underline} political and security
[organisations]{.underline}.

[In NATO]{.underline} and the EU's [responses to **hybrid threats**, OS
emerges as the actor's ability to **tolerate**, and
**cope**]{.underline} with change (Browning and Joenniemi 2017, p. 32).
Albeit seemingly counterintuitive due to the general human preference
for stability over change, OS might nonetheless "derive\[\] from
constructive attempts to (re)create and consolidate collective
self-identities" (Vieira 2016, p. 292). [The **leitmotif** of adapting
to]{.underline} changing [circumstances is reflected in]{.underline}
both the EU and [NATO's]{.underline} notable [emphases on
**resilience**]{.underline} -- which, I argue, could be regarded as an
institutional alias for their positive sense of self, and by consequence
a functional equivalent of these actors' OS. [Rendered as "the
**ability** of **states** and societies to **reform**]{.underline}, thus
[withstanding]{.underline} and recovering from internal and external
**[crisis]{.underline}**" (Wagner and Anholt 2016, p. 414),
"[resilience]{.underline}" [manifests a claim on]{.underline} a sense of
[institutional **self-worth** and **relevance** amidst **deep
uncertainty**. Invoking "**resilience**" appears]{.underline}
emphatically [as an **anxiety management**]{.underline} rather than
avoidance **[exercise]{.underline}**, at relatively low ontological
costs involved for the actors concerned. [Resilience]{.underline} is
thus conceptually linked, yet not synonymous with OS: it [functions as
an **imperfect solution** to the]{.underline} perennial [**ontological
insecurity** problem]{.underline} -- for OS remains always to be
measured in degrees, rather than being categorically achievable in
practice. As a notoriously flexible notion, politically convenient and
intellectually perplexing in equal doses, resilience refers to "the
process of seeking to maintain the status quo in the face of shocks, but
it also refers \[to\] the idea of transforming a referent object"
(Bourbeau and Ryan 2018, p. 223).

**[Adaptability]{.underline}**, or the ability to cope with change, [has
been the **key trope** in NATO's discursive
self-presentation]{.underline} throughout the post-Cold War era (Barany
and Rauchhaus 2011). Likewise, the EU Global Strategy on foreign and
security policy (2016) demonstrates the Union's shift in emphasising
"resilience" over more progressive foreign policy goals, as reflected in
the earlier, 2003 EU Security Strategy (Mälksoo 2016, Wagner and Anholt
2016, Juncos 2017). NATO and the EU's institutional emphasis on
resilience captures the paradoxical dynamic of the "strategy of doing"
in their OS maximisation attempts, combining a continuous struggle to
manage emergent change by routinised practices, yet also demonstrating
ability to undertake action to tackle transformative change (see
Flockhart 2016, p. 816). For what it is worth,
"[resilience]{.underline}" [functions as a **symbolic codename**
for]{.underline} the EU and [NATO's institutional responses to
the]{.underline} deeply [unsettling **ontological insecurity** condition
evoked by **hybrid threats**/**warfare**. It captures the]{.underline}
necessarily "**[hybrid]{.underline}**" **[defence]{.underline}**
[to]{.underline} effectively [counter]{.underline} [the
**menace**]{.underline} in question, including such non-traditional
issues as social and political cohesion, vigilance about the funding
sources of domestic political parties, and legitimate and effective
governance in its spectrum of security (Galeotti 2015, 2016a).
[Countering hybrid threats by propping]{.underline} the EU and [NATO's
**resilience**]{.underline} in various areas ([ranging from **critical
infrastructure**, **energy** and **cyber security**]{.underline} to
transport[, **financial system** and **society** as a
whole]{.underline}) [enables]{.underline} a host of [claims]{.underline}
[on the pertinent polities' **viability**, ability to **adjust** to
the]{.underline} quickly changing demands of the **[modern
world]{.underline}**, ownership of the contemporary security scene, and
last but not least a workable partnership between the two organisations.
Understood "as a preventive and deterrent action to solidify societies
and avoid escalation of crises both within and outside the EU" (European
Commission 2017), resilience, and the calls to strengthen it, put the
main responsibility to the respective member states and their populace,
but notably also partner countries in neighbourhood regions (European
Commission and High Representative 2017). [The]{.underline} vernacular
[targets of **hybrid threats**]{.underline} thus [become]{.underline}
the main [stakeholders in]{.underline} the [OS management pursuits
of]{.underline} these [Western organisations]{.underline}, effectively
[enabling]{.underline} the EU and [NATO's evasion of responsibility
under the banner of **sought OS provision**]{.underline}.

### Link\-\--Hybrid War\-\--AT: Offense

#### Hybrid war is [descriptively false]{.underline}\-\--information warfare and cyberwar have no [tangible effects]{.underline}. 

Jyri **Raitasalo 17**, Lieutenant Colonel Raitasalo, PhD, is a senior
staff officer at the Finnish Ministry of Defence Planning Unit
(strategic planning) and was head lecturer of strategy at the Finnish
National Defence University, 2017, "Getting a Grip on the So-Called
'Hybrid Warfare'," ASPJ Africa & Francophonie,
https://www.airuniversity.af.edu/Portals/10/ASPJ_French/journals_E/Volume-08_Issue-3/raitasalo_e.pdf

The Emergence of the Hybrid War Thesis

[Hybrid war]{.underline} and hybrid warfare [represent the **latest
manifestation** of the]{.underline} Western [need to
(re)conceptualise]{.underline} and (re)define the post-Cold War
[international security]{.underline} logic and associated [rules
